Supreme Court invalidates most of President Trump&#39;s tariffs

ABC News(19.4M) ·27:45 ·158.8K views · 2026-02-20
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
The Supreme Court ruled 6-3 that President Trump lacks the unilateral authority to impose most global tariffs, striking down a cornerstone of his second-term economic agenda and forcing the potential refund of over $140 billion in collected revenue.
TL;DR The Supreme Court ruled 6-3 that President Trump lacks the unilateral authority to impose most global tariffs, striking down a cornerstone of his second-term economic agenda and forcing the potential refund of over $140 billion in collected revenue. Constitutional...

TL;DR

The Supreme Court ruled 6-3 that President Trump lacks the unilateral authority to impose most global tariffs, striking down a cornerstone of his second-term economic agenda and forcing the potential refund of over $140 billion in collected revenue.

Legal Basis and the "Separation of Powers"

  • Constitutional Authority: The Court ruled that under the Constitution, the power to impose duties and taxes belongs to Congress, not the Executive branch.
  • IEEPA Rejection: The administration relied on the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) of 1977, arguing it allowed for tariffs during "unusual and extraordinary threats."
      • Chief Justice John Roberts’ majority opinion clarified that while the act allows the President to "regulate importation," it does not explicitly mention tariffs.
      • The Court found that chronic economic conditions—such as trade imbalances—do not constitute the "unusual" emergencies required by the statute.
  • A Non-Partisan Rebuke: Despite a conservative majority, Trump appointees Justice Amy Coney Barrett and Justice Neil Gorsuch joined the liberal wing and Chief Justice Roberts in the majority.
      • Dissenting Opinions: Justices Thomas, Alito, and Kavanaugh dissented, with Kavanaugh arguing the ruling would create a "mess" by upending trade deals and economic stability.

Economic Fallout and the $142 Billion Refund

  • Revenue at Risk: The Trump administration has collected approximately $142 billion in tariff revenue during the first year of his second term; 70% of all issued tariffs are affected by this ruling.
  • Refund Complexity: Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent has signaled that returning this money will be a "slow, drawn-out process."
      • Claimants: Large corporations like Costco and coalitions of small businesses have already filed for refunds.
      • Logistics: The government may use Customs and Border Protection portals to return fees, but the math regarding exactly how much is owed to whom remains a significant challenge.
  • Market Reaction: The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose nearly 200 points following the news. Investors generally view the removal of sweeping tariffs as a reduction in overhead and "uncertainty" for business models.

Impact on Foreign Policy and Trade Leverage

  • Loss of Negotiating Power: President Trump utilized tariffs as his primary "leverage" and "pressure point" to negotiate trade frameworks, lower prescription drug costs, and even influence foreign leadership (e.g., Brazil’s treatment of former President Bolsonaro).
  • Treaty Validity: Roughly a dozen trade deals or frameworks negotiated under the threat of these tariffs may now require formal congressional authorization to remain or become valid.
  • Administration "Plan B": The White House has suggested it will seek "workarounds," such as using Section 232 authorities (national security) which were not struck down. These authorities allow for more targeted tariffs on specific industries like steel and aluminum.

Political Context and the State of the Union

  • State of the Union Timing: The decision arrives just days before the State of the Union address. President Trump is expected to address the justices directly, having previously warned that striking down tariffs would have "devastating consequences."
  • Executive Power Limits: Analysts describe this as the most significant "marker" the Court has placed against Trump’s expansive view of presidential power, following a previous ruling that blocked his deployment of the National Guard for domestic crime-fighting.
  • Legacy of the Roberts Court: Legal contributors noted that Chief Justice Roberts appears to be "shrinking the presidency" to force the executive branch to compromise with Congress rather than ruling by decree.

Notable Quotes

"This is a massive, devastating, and consequential blow for this administration. It's also a rare rebuke of Trump by this Supreme Court, something that we haven't really seen so far much in this second term."

— Mary Bruce, Chief White House Correspondent

"The ruling says this kind of a scheme can't be implemented without going through bicameral passage in Congress... shortcuts often come with consequences."

— James Sample, ABC News Legal Contributor

"This president has seen tariffs as an instrument that goes far beyond economics as a way to pressure countries to do what he wants them to do... That authority has now been struck down."

— John Karl, Chief Washington Correspondent

"The court is simply not going to be refereeing these fights. Work it out with Congress, compromise with the other side to get legislation passed."

— Sarah Isgur, ABC News Legal Analyst

Big picture, we’re seeing Chief Justice John Roberts navigate the Supreme Court through treacherous times and partisan times, where both parties are trying to pull the court into their food fights. We saw during the Biden administration when the court struck down their student loan debt forgiveness executive action, this is very similar in terms of the reasoning of the court. It is saying you can't go back to an old, vague statute that Congress passed decades ago and pour into it new, big executive power. If you want to have your policy agenda, you need to work through Congress to do it. The court is simply not going to be refereeing these fights. A lot of investors, traders, and analysts did expect this could be coming. They thought that the justification for these tariffs from the president would get struck down, so that might be why you're not seeing, you know, say a 500-point move higher in the Dow right now. The Supreme Court just struck down most of President Trump's global tariffs. It's a significant loss for the president on the centerpiece of his economic agenda. The president had said a ruling against him would be an economic body blow to the country. And now the justices are defying him in a landmark ruling saying he doesn't have the authority to impose those tariffs. Let’s get right to ABC senior Washington reporter Devin Dwyer, who covers the Supreme Court for us. Hi Devin. So the Trump administration argued that the International Emergency Economic Powers Act authorizes the president to impose tariffs on any country at any level. How are the justices responding to that in this ruling? They simply rejected that argument, Diane. It was an unprecedented claim. No president has ever done what Donald Trump did with tariffs, collecting more than $142 billion over the first year in office here in this second term. 70% of the tariffs that he's issued were implicated in this case, and today they were struck down. The court's decision was really a dispute about two words: "regulate importation." When Congress wrote the law in 1977, this act that you referenced, it gave the president some power to regulate importation of goods into the country when there's an emergency. But they never mentioned tariffs. And so what the justices did in dispute, really, because there were three dissenting, is what those words mean. Justices Clarence Thomas, Samuel Alito, and Brett Kavanaugh believe that regulate importation is broad enough to include tariffs. They thought that the president should have that power. But clearly a majority of the court, six justices including two appointed by Donald Trump, Amy Coney Barrett and Neil Gorsuch, said that’s simply not the case. Congress would never have tucked in such a momentous power into a law in 1977 without spelling it out. And so Chief Justice John Roberts in his opinion said the law simply doesn't support what the president has asserted and therefore these tariffs are illegal, Diane. And I want to bring in ABC News legal contributor James Sample on this because, James, in the decision, the Chief Justice writes that the president must identify clear congressional authorization to exercise his authority on tariffs. What's your take on how they wrote this opinion? Well Diane, I think what Devin said is absolutely spot on. And the other thing I would emphasize is that to a certain extent, as complicated and as much legalese as there is in a 170-page opinion from the Supreme Court, there's also a very simple, almost elementary school lesson, which is that shortcuts often come with consequences. I think what the court is saying here is that by imposing these tariffs without involving Congress, when Congress has the power to tax and Congress has the power to spend and a tariff functions very much like a tax, it is almost inconceivable according to the majority that the founders would allow a president to use vague, ambiguous authority to impose extraordinary economic consequences very much akin to a tax. The framers of the Constitution that gave those powers specifically to Congress wouldn't allow a logical stretch of existing statutory authority here. And so I think what the court is saying here is it's not that this kind of a tariff scheme couldn't be implemented. That's not what the ruling says. It's that the ruling says this kind of a scheme can't be implemented without going through bicameral passage in Congress and presentment to the president. Selina, the court is supposed to be apolitical, but there are political ramifications here. What do you make of that aspect of this given this is a rare rebuke of the president by this court? Yeah Diane, I mean this is a huge blow. It's hard to overstate just how central this tariff policy was not only to the president's economic policy but also to his foreign policy and diplomacy overseas. The president really wielded these tariffs as a tool to negotiate with other countries. And for months now, the Trump administration has been warning that this will be extremely damaging for America on the global stage if the Supreme Court were to take away this power from the president. For instance, we have seen Trump use tariffs as a negotiating tool to force foreign companies to manufacture here in America, to extract concessions, and to force them to invest in America. So this is a major blow for the Trump administration that goes far beyond tariffs and the question of whether or not they're going to have to pay back these billions of dollars to these importers. In addition to that, though, the administration has known that this could be a possibility. They have been repeatedly saying for weeks now that they have a plan B. They’ve got an alternative. We have heard the U.S. Trade Representative talk about how quickly they're going to move to replace these tariffs with other authorities that are not under IEEPA. For instance, there are the Section 232 tariffs that they've used on all sorts of things like steel, aluminum, and auto tariffs. They could use that to cover other sectors. But it's unclear, Diane, how long it will take for them to replace those tariffs. This is certainly a more complicated authority than IEEPA, so still a huge blow, but this is not the last that we're going to hear from this administration about tariffs. And I want to bring in ABC’s Elizabeth Schulze on the immediate impact because, Elizabeth, what happens now? The government’s estimated to have collected over $100 billion from these tariffs. So how do you refund that? That is the key question—is how is this going to work, Diane. And in the Supreme Court decision, we see this laid out pretty much saying this is likely to be a mess. This says that refunds of billions of dollars would have significant consequences for the U.S. Treasury. An estimate we have from the Yale Budget Lab says we're talking about $142 billion. So what we've seen when we've talked to businesses, Diane, is that a lot of these costs are basically fees that are automatically deducted in your shipping costs when you bring that product in. Do they use that same kind of infrastructure through the Customs and Border Protection online portal to send that money back to businesses that they at first took away? So that is kind of one option when we talk to businesses; that's how they think it might happen. The question of where this gets really messy is: how much are we talking about? Who's doing the math to calculate where that money is going to go and how much exactly is owed? And then as businesses are waiting for that, keep in mind that doesn't necessarily mean that consumers are going to get that money either. These are costs that businesses have already paid. If they've been passed down to customers, those are things you've already paid. We’re talking about about $1,700 for the typical U.S. household from tariffs. Though if these tariffs go away, that number does get significantly lower. Economists over at Yale Budget Lab say that without IEEPA tariffs—these ones that were struck down—we're looking at closer to maybe $500 or $600 for the typical cost of President Trump's tariffs on U.S. households. And I want to bring in our chief Washington correspondent John Karl for a bit of the bigger picture here. What is the bigger impact here for the president, John, given how frequently he leaned on the threat of tariffs to negotiate other deals? I mean, it is a monumental setback for Donald Trump. I really don't think we can overstate it in a couple of ways here. First of all, just consider the Supreme Court itself. Trump has often acted as if at least the justices that he put on the Supreme Court should be doing his bidding. And the Supreme Court has backed him up in a number of significant ways, of course, over the past year. But this, I would argue, is the most important decision for Trump's policy overall, and the Supreme Court said flatly no. An overwhelming decision, 6-3, with two of the justices he put on the court doing that. And it comes not long after the Supreme Court also struck down his ability to send in the National Guard to cities to basically participate in crime-fighting operations. So this shows that this Supreme Court is not simply a rubber stamp for Donald Trump. More broadly on the issue of tariffs, Donald Trump has seen tariffs as central to his presidency. And by the way, this is one of the issues that he has been consistent on throughout his time in politics and before he ever ran for office. His belief that tariffs should be imposed on America's trading partners because in his view, America's getting a raw deal. Well, what he learned in this decision is something that you could have read right in the Constitution: that the Constitution gives Congress the power to impose duties and taxes, not the president. And he was going about imposing tariffs on virtually every country in the world, negotiating trade deals and putting them in force without any congressional approval or authorization. Those now have to be unwound, it seems to me. Not just the refunds that will theoretically need to be given to companies and individuals that have already paid tariffs, but what about the about a dozen trade deals that Trump has negotiated or frameworks that he has spent time negotiating? It seems to me that all of those would now need, or most of them at least would now need, congressional authorization to go into effect. That is the way things usually work. When you think about the big trade deals that have been negotiated over the past several decades, the Mexico-Canada Free Trade Agreement that Trump himself negotiated in his first term was approved by Congress. It replaced NAFTA, which was approved by Congress and didn't go into effect until it was approved by Congress. So there are major implications for the president's economic policy, for what he can expect from this Supreme Court, and for the way he has conducted foreign policy as well. And Devin, you say the dissent in this ruling is notable. Why? Well, first of all, it involves one of President Trump's hand-picked justices, as John Karl was just speaking about. It's extraordinary in its own right on something of this scale to see him support the president—only one of them there, Brett Kavanaugh, in his corner. The two others, of course, sided with the majority. So that was sort of significant to see. A lot of people expected the Trump appointees would side with Trump. But what's also notable here, Diane, is how much Brett Kavanaugh's dissent leans into the practical implications. Nowhere in the court's decision do they talk about the practical impact of having to refund all these tariffs. They don't mention what it will mean for the economy, the government, the trade deals, or the foreign negotiations. But Brett Kavanaugh does. And he really emphasizes the point that this is going to be a mess. It could upend trade deals, and there's really no way of gauging the entirety of the scope of this thing in the short term. That was one of the factors he indicated for why he would have upheld the tariffs, and he eventually lost that vote. James, you also say this ruling will be a big part of Justice Roberts’ legacy. How so? Well Diane, to the point that Chief Justice Roberts here, the Supreme Court has been extraordinarily willing during the Roberts era to uphold presidential discretion. And that's been especially true in matters of national security. If you think about from travel bans to border emergencies, the Roberts court has shown a readiness to grant presidents wide, wide latitude in matters that involve foreign affairs abroad that it does usually deny to domestic agencies. Chief Justice Roberts has been very much on board with that trend. But here, this is him drawing a line. Even though the administration argued that IEEPA allows presidential action in response to an unusual and extraordinary threat, the court disagreed. The administration argued that the trade imbalances constituted just such a threat and that modern globalized supply chains have turned trade imbalances into genuine security risks. But the court and Chief Justice Roberts draw a line and say, look, chronic economic conditions are not unusual. While they may be serious, they're not unusual because they are in fact chronic. And Chief Justice Roberts draws a line with respect to presidential authority—and not a line that he has typically been known for—but here he sets a very firm precedent. Vinod Khosla: AI Tutors, Doctors &amp; Farmer Experts For 1.5B Indians | AI Summit 2026

Business Today(3.0M) ·12:18 ·141 views · 2026-02-20
Vinod Khosla (Founder, Khosla Ventures)
Vinod Khosla advocates for the immediate deployment of AI-driven tutors, doctors, and agronomists integrated into India’s Aadhaar ecosystem to provide high-level expert services to the bottom half of the population at near-zero cost.
TL;DR Vinod Khosla advocates for the immediate deployment of AI-driven tutors, doctors, and agronomists integrated into India’s Aadhaar ecosystem to provide high-level expert services to the bottom half of the population at near-zero cost. Personalized Learning: Khosla argues...

TL;DR

Vinod Khosla advocates for the immediate deployment of AI-driven tutors, doctors, and agronomists integrated into India’s Aadhaar ecosystem to provide high-level expert services to the bottom half of the population at near-zero cost.

Revolutionizing Education via AI Tutors

  • Personalized Learning: Khosla argues that AI tutors are superior to human tutors because they use "knowledge tracing" to assess a student's specific gaps within minutes and tailor instruction accordingly.
  • Existing Scale: Through ck-12.org, over 400 million students globally and 4 million in India have already used these AI-based services.
  • Diksha 3.0: Khosla proposes upgrading India's current "Diksha" content library—which he describes as currently "unusable"—into an AI-first experience in partnership with Sarvam AI.
      • The system is already compatible with CBSE and National Education Policy standards.
      • Services are available in multiple Indic languages, including Hindi, Odia, and others.

Democratizing Healthcare with AI Doctors

  • Comprehensive Primary Care: AI can provide 24/7 expertise in disease management, mental health, physical therapy, and nutrition coaching at a level of comprehensiveness currently unavailable even in Western countries.
  • Solving the Doctor Shortage: Khosla notes that India cannot reach Western doctor-patient ratios through traditional means, even with a trillion dollars and decades of time; AI is the only viable way to scale resources.
  • The "Intern" Implementation Model: He suggests a 1–2 year transition where AI acts as an "MBBS graduate intern" overseen by human physicians before moving toward more autonomous operation.
      • The AI can diagnose, prescribe tests, and handle dialogue, only triaging to humans for physical exams or emergencies.

AI Agronomy for Rural Farmers

  • PhD-Level Expertise: The goal is to provide every farmer with a PhD-level agronomist available locally on their specific small plot of land.
  • Low Barrier to Entry: Recognizing literacy challenges, these systems are designed to function via voice and image recognition.
      • Farmers can simply speak to the AI or take pictures of their crops to receive expert advice.

The Infrastructure Proposal: AI as a Public Utility

  • Integration with Aadhaar/UPI: Khosla proposes that these AI services be integrated into the Aadhaar identity system, similar to how UPI revolutionized digital payments.
  • Non-Profit Delivery: He plans to build a "Section 8" non-profit company to build, operate, and eventually transfer these systems into the state-run Aadhaar ecosystem.
  • Localized Adaptation: The systems require iterative cycles to adapt to specific Indian conditions, local languages, and regional disease patterns.

***

Notable Quotes

"This is not helping a doctor, this is building a doctor." — Vinod Khosla
"Rich people can afford personal tutors. They won't do as well as people who have access to this AI." — Vinod Khosla
"There's very little a human doctor can do that this AI can't do today, other than the physical parts. If they have to feel your stomach, of course, an AI can't do that yet." — Vinod Khosla
"Unless AI benefits the bottom half of the Indian population, we're not going to see a huge amount of impact." — Vinod Khosla
Good afternoon. Today, I want to discuss immediate applications of AI that can benefit the bottom half of the Indian population. Rather than focusing on business or technology trends, I want to address what can be done within the next year or two to reach 1.5 billion people with impactful, immediate benefits. For AI to truly matter in India, it must serve those who need it most. We can now provide 24/7 personal tutors for every child, nearly free medical care through AI doctors, and PhD-level agronomy expertise for every farmer. These services do not even require literacy; users can simply speak or share pictures to access them. In rural India, many children lack educational support because teachers are often unavailable. AI-based tutors ensure that every child has their destiny in their own hands. The non-profit ck-12.org already provides free AI content and tutors globally, reaching 400 million students. In India, millions of students are already benefiting from these services, which are fully compatible with the CBSE curriculum and National Education Policy standards. These AI tutors are efficacious and, in many ways, superior to human tutors. They can assess a student's level within minutes and use knowledge tracing to specifically target gaps in understanding. We are currently working with Sarvam to develop Diksha 3.0, an AI-first version of India’s existing content library. By leveraging billions of student questions, this model knows how to teach effectively and includes professional development for teachers to keep pace with modern education. AI doctors represent a similar transformation. This technology can provide 24/7 primary care, chronic disease management, mental health therapy, and nutrition coaching at a trivial cost. This level of comprehensive care often surpasses what is available even in the United States or other Western countries. While humans are still necessary for physical examinations and surgeries, AI can handle the majority of primary care functions and triage patients to human specialists when needed. Historically, scaling India’s doctor-patient ratio to Western levels was considered impossible, even with decades of time and trillions of dollars. However, AI allows India to leapfrog traditional systems and achieve a superior standard of care. The proposed model involves the AI initially acting as a supervised intern, assisting MBBS graduates, until the need for human oversight eventually diminishes. These services—education, healthcare, and agronomy—should be integrated into the Aadhaar ecosystem, similar to the UPI system. Every farmer should have access to PhD-level agronomists locally and 24/7. To achieve this, I am proposing the creation of a Section 8 non-profit company to build, operate, and eventually transfer these systems into the Aadhaar infrastructure. While these systems require iterations to adapt to Indian languages and regional disease patterns, the core technology is ready today. We can multiply our medical and educational resources by organizing vast, previously unusable libraries of information and making them accessible and relevant via AI. The future is here. Massive impact services that once required hundreds of billions of dollars can now be implemented cheaply and at scale. By scaling medicine, teaching, and agronomy, we can provide the bottom half of the population with the tools they need to succeed. This is an incredible opportunity for India, and failing to act on it would be a significant loss.

Flight to Safety Begins, Private Credit Cracks & the Software Bloodbath Continues | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(154.0K) ·24:41 ·78.8K views · 2026-02-20
Steve Eisman (Host, The Weekly Wrap)

TL;DR The market is shifting from performance chasing to a flight to safety, evidenced by a 20% collapse in software and a surge in consumer staples. Significant liquidity risks are emerging in private credit and life insurance due to duration mismatches and hidden leverage,...

TL;DR

The market is shifting from performance chasing to a flight to safety, evidenced by a 20% collapse in software and a surge in consumer staples. Significant liquidity risks are emerging in private credit and life insurance due to duration mismatches and hidden leverage, while even retail giants like Walmart are signaling a weakening U.S. consumer.

Market Volatility and the "Software Bloodbath"

  • Flight to Safety: While the broader market remains flat year-to-date, there is a distinct rotation under the hood. The S&P 500 Info Tech sector is down 4%, while the Software subsector has plummeted 20% due to AI-related fears.
  • Staples Rotation: In contrast, the Consumer Staples sector is up 13%. Eisman notes the irony that staples' fundamentals have not actually improved—they are still suffering from inflation and tariffs—but are being used as a defensive hedge.
  • Software Earnings Pain: The narrative in software is currently so negative that stocks are sold off regardless of news quality.
      • Palo Alto Networks: Despite beating revenue and EPS expectations, the stock fell over 6% after hours due to mixed forward guidance.
  • The Payments Duopoly: Eisman argues that Mastercard and Visa are the only viable long-term investments in the payments space. Other players like Global Payments are currently "trading vehicles" at best, characterized by high volatility and low revenue growth.

Emerging Risks: Private Credit and Life Insurance

  • Private Credit "Ticking Time Bomb": Reports that Blue Owl is restricting withdrawals highlight a classic duration mismatch.
      • Liquidity Mismatch: Private equity firms have sold private credit funds to retail investors with quarterly redemption rights, yet the underlying loans are illiquid and long-term.
      • Inherent Instability: Eisman compares this to the Savings and Loan crisis of the 1980s, predicting more funds will suspend redemptions as investors grow nervous.
  • Life Insurance Scandal: Eisman warns of a brewing crisis in the life insurance sector involving increasing investment risk and "hidden leverage" driven by private equity involvement (e.g., Athene/Apollo). He views this as a potential systemic risk that requires forensic scrutiny.

Corporate M&A and Valuation Lessons

  • Warner Bros / Paramount: Eisman is skeptical of the reopened negotiations, stating Warner Bros is not worth $70 billion and suffers from a fractured internal culture. He suggests that Netflix may actually benefit by "losing" the deal to Paramount.
  • Proprietary Data Moats: Moody’s outperformed S&P because of its proprietary database, which protects its ratings franchise from AI disruption. Eisman favors companies that function as duopolies with high barriers to entry.
  • Homebuilder Validation: The acquisition of TPH by Sumitomo Forestry at 1.3x tangible book value provides "outside affirmation" for the homebuilder sector, specifically validating Eisman's position in Meritage (MTH).

Macro Outlook: Recession and the Consumer

  • The Stressed Consumer: Walmart’s weak guidance (projecting only 6% EPS growth) suggests the U.S. consumer is finally faltering. Walmart’s high P/E (50x) is attributed to "scarcity value," as it is one of the few traditional retailers still performing relatively well.
  • Recession as a "Reset": Eisman argues that a full recession may be the only way to break the "buy the dip" mentality of retail investors.
      • Recalibration: A recession cleanses the economy by weeding out companies with weak balance sheets and forcing a market correction that fundamental analysis—rather than momentum—can eventually lead out of.
  • AI Infrastructure Play: Quanta (PWR) remains a top pick as a "major beneficiary" of the AI data center boom. As utilities scale up to meet power demands, engineering and construction firms like Quanta see massive revenue growth (up 20% in Q4).

Those were the news reports. And what is this all about? Private equity has been selling private credit funds to retail investors for years. This has, in my view, always been a ticking time bomb. Retail investors are allowed to redeem quarterly, but the loans that the funds make are both illiquid and long term. This is a classic duration mismatch. It's what destroyed the savings and loan industry in the 1980s. Duration mismatches always, always create problems. It's only a question of when. Here, retail investors are getting increasingly nervous about the loans the private equity industry has been making. The publicity has been bad for over a year, and so they are putting in for withdrawals. However, the loans are too illiquid to meet these redemption notices. This was always inevitable. Don't be surprised if over the next few months, other private credit funds suspend redemptions as well. This story is far, far from over. Exclusive: Kristalina Georgieva, MD, IMF On AI, Growth &amp; India’s Global Rise

Business Today(3.0M) ·29:46 ·1.9K views · 2026-02-20
Kristalina Georgieva (Managing Director, IMF)
IMF Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva projects that AI could boost global growth by 0.8%, but warns of a "tsunami" hitting labor markets that requires India’s model of "democratized" digital infrastructure to prevent widening global inequality.
TL;DR IMF Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva projects that AI could boost global growth by 0.8%, but warns of a "tsunami" hitting labor markets that requires India’s model of "democratized" digital infrastructure to prevent widening global inequality. Economic Growth...

TL;DR

IMF Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva projects that AI could boost global growth by 0.8%, but warns of a "tsunami" hitting labor markets that requires India’s model of "democratized" digital infrastructure to prevent widening global inequality.

AI’s Impact on Global Growth and Labor

  • Economic Growth Catalyst: AI has the potential to break the current cycle of "anemic" 3.3% global growth and high debt, potentially lifting global output by 0.8% and returning the world to pre-pandemic performance levels.
  • Labor Market Disruption: Georgieva describes AI as a "tsunami" hitting the labor market.
      • Impact Statistics: Approximately 40% of jobs globally—and 60% in advanced economies—will be impacted (either enhanced or replaced) in the coming years.
      • The "Middle Squeeze": AI is creating high-paying jobs for those with enhanced skills and increasing demand for low-skill service jobs (restaurants, hotels), but it is "squeezing" middle-tier jobs that are neither enhanced nor service-oriented.
  • Policy Recommendations: To manage the transition, Georgieva advocates for:
      • Redefining Education: Shifting focus toward "learning to learn" and adaptability.
      • "Flexicurity": Adopting the Danish model of combining labor market flexibility with strong social safety nets to protect displaced workers.

India’s Model of Digital Democratization

  • Digital Public Infrastructure (DPI): India’s investment in DPI, including digital IDs for 1.4 billion people, provides a foundational "stack" that makes AI more accessible than the corporate-driven models of the West.
  • Democratizing AI: Unlike models that may widen the "accordion of opportunities" between the haves and have-nots, India’s focus on applications for public goods (healthcare, education) is viewed as a blueprint for the Global South.
  • Leadership in Innovation: India’s shift toward reducing regulatory burdens is praised, though Georgieva notes that a "culture of creating space for innovation" must be balanced with ethical guardrails to protect human dignity.

Global Economic Risks and Fiscal Challenges

  • Exhausted Fiscal Buffers: A primary concern is that many countries have exhausted their fiscal capacity to respond to future shocks due to high debt levels incurred during the pandemic and inflation spikes.
  • The "Solidarity Gap": In a more "shock-prone" world (climate, conflict, pandemics), Georgieva worries that rich countries may no longer be able to provide the same level of financial support to poorer nations, making regional solidarity and IMF programs (currently 50 active) critical.
  • Climate Leadership: Georgieva highlights the International Solar Alliance as a key example of how India can lead on climate issues, which she identifies as a major risk to economic security for vulnerable regions.

The Future of Global Trade and Multipolarity

  • Trade Resilience: Despite rising tariffs and fragmentation, Georgieva argues that trade is "like water" and will find ways around obstacles through regional and plurilateral agreements.
  • Shift to Multipolarity: The world is moving away from uniform global trade rules toward a more complex multipolar reality. Georgieva suggests that supply chains will continue to cross borders because total self-reliance is economically impossible.
  • South Asian Integration: There is significant untapped potential for India to lead deeper trade integration within South Asia to bolster regional economic security.
"AI is like a tsunami hitting the labor market... We risk falling behind if we don't lean forward with everything we have." — Kristalina Georgieva
"What India is doing in AI is phenomenal because the goal of India is to democratize AI. The haves and the have-nots may have a big distance in between; what India is saying is it need not be that way." — Kristalina Georgieva
"Trade is like water: you put an obstacle, it goes around it... There is no going back to some place where everybody relies only on its own capacity." — Kristalina Georgieva
Today is a very special guest: the managing director of the International Monetary Fund, Kristalina Georgieva. Welcome to the show. You are here at the AI Impact Summit in New Delhi, a massive gathering. And given all that you've been seeing and observing, I want your first key takeaways about the AI summit and what do you think it has really delivered so far? Let me start with Namaste. Thank you very much. I have to respond in kind. It is great to be in India, especially for an event so dynamic and inspiring. What the summit does is to bring not just the world together, but to bring the developing world in India to meet with those that are ahead on this journey. What India is doing in AI is phenomenal because the goal of India is to democratize AI. What is the biggest risk we face? That AI is going to open the accordion of opportunities too widely. The haves and the have-nots may have a big distance in between. What India is saying is it need not be that way. We can work to make AI a source of benefit for the whole of society. And we India are determined to help the rest of the world, especially the Global South, to benefit from AI. My two big takeaways: one, AI is already here and the speed with which it is affecting the way we work, we live, and we interact with each other is so high—highest in the history of technological transformation. We risk falling behind if we don't lean forward with everything we have. Two, it is a tremendous source of excitement and opportunity, but it also brings risks. And we have to embrace the opportunities of higher productivity and higher growth, while managing the risks, especially the risks of displacement of workers. And that, for us at the IMF, is a very important objective: to help our members grab the opportunities and manage the risks. And here in India, during the AI summit, I learned a lot about ways we can do it. On the labor front, and you're an economist all your life, you've studied this issue. How significant is this risk? Because for the developing world, labor displacement will be a bigger risk, one would imagine, than in other parts of the world. So, what we know is the following. Already there is evidence that the impact on the labor market is very significant. I mean, AI is like a tsunami hitting the labor market. We assess 40% of jobs globally and 60% in advanced economies to be impacted by AI in the next years, either enhanced, made more productive, or replaced. And when we study concretely what is happening, we find a very interesting story. Already in the United States, one in ten jobs require AI-enhanced skills, and it pays more. People in these jobs with more money in their pockets spend more, and that creates demand for low-skill workers in restaurants, in hotels, and in entertainment. The total impact on employment, surprisingly in areas we studied, is positive. But it is positive because of the increase of low-paying jobs. So what is happening is we have one part of the labor force getting better pay because of better skills, and another part of the labor force getting jobs but with less pay. In these low-paying jobs, who is squeezed? The middle. The jobs in the middle that are neither enhanced nor created as low service jobs, they get squeezed in relative terms. We also see automation eliminating entry-level jobs. So recent graduates worry: where is the job for me? And this is where the attention of policymakers needs to go. We are not helpless. What we can do is first, redefine education for the era of AI. Learn to learn and be adaptable in terms of how you adjust to jobs. Two, put in place good social safety nets. So if people for a while are displaced, they have something to lean on for themselves and their families. There is a very good system, Flexicurity, in Denmark. I highly recommend that for this world of AI, we create that flexibility in the labor market. And actually, congratulations to India for the labor market reforms, but we must combine that flexibility with security and with helping people to adjust. That's a very good prescription that might be needed in the days ahead. Just one more follow-up on the subject of AI, and this is to do with the AI arms race—these are my words, but you understand this as an economist. The oil economy had haves and have-nots. We've seen that in other spheres. The United States is advocating that the globe should use the American stack. China has a certain stack. India is going down a different path. What is your own assessment of the path that India seems to be choosing? That is to use what exists elsewhere, including a foundational model that has just been announced in India, and build applications for public good, health care, and education. Is that the right way for a country like India? For India, this is the right way. Why? India invested over the last years in digital public infrastructure. It is the foundation for AI to be more accessible to more people and to be applicable in more areas. India created digital ID for 1.4 billion people, and that gave a big boost to the digital economy. Now comes AI, and to make it beneficial for more people, it is appropriate to think of models that are specifically designed for a sector or for a particular activity. And that democratization of AI is what I started with; it is impressive in India. Now, where the world is headed, inevitably there will be different paths. And the issue is: how about the developing world, the countries that risk falling behind? And there, I think the model that India is developing for India may be more applicable than the high-tech corporate model of the United States. What matters today is that we think ahead, independent on which path is chosen. What should we be most worried about? And frankly, I think our biggest worry should be guardrails. We want AI for good, not for evil. We want AI that protects the dignity and the rights of humans. And that requires more attention to the ethical foundation of AI and much more attention to what are the guardrails we have to collectively as humanity put in place. And there, engagements like this summit are very helpful. They bring the tech world with policymakers and leaders from different countries, and they create space to think through how to go about a transformative technology that brings enormous opportunities. We see this potentially lifting global growth by 0.8%. Just to give the audience a sense of what that means: it means that we will have growth globally above the pre-pandemic level. Right now it's anemic at 3.3%. We can break out of this path of low growth and high debt with AI. But for productivity growth and economic growth to benefit the world, we have to pay attention to the risks and we have to work together as a global community. This is in a world that is more fragmented today than it was before the AI revolution. But equally, if guardrails are necessary, and one would agree with you, how do you ensure that innovation doesn't get curbed due to regulation or over-regulation? Well, that is, that is the trickiest question. I think the only answer to the question is to work together and identify what are the biggest risks and how we can put guardrails for those, and do that very carefully. Because you're right, innovation is what drives prosperity, and we have seen far too many times suffocating innovation with excessive regulation. India over the last years has worked hard to reduce the regulatory burden. I would argue that this is an area where more can be done to implement the great reforms that India has mustered over the last years. And I think that culture of creating space for innovation, but managing risks that could come from bad behavior, that balance is one that we need to aspire for. It's not an easy one, but it can be done. It can be done when we all work together. And to finish on that point, many thanks to Prime Minister Modi and to India for bringing us for this AI summit here in Delhi. Can and should Europe also deregulate? So, in Europe, I, I happen to know Europe very well. I was EU commissioner and vice president of the European Commission for a while. In Europe, the understanding now is that there is red tape that needs to be taken out. But Europe thinks about it as efficient regulation and effective regulation. The term "deregulation" they avoid using because they don't want to send the signal that everything goes. They want to ensure there are protections for workers and for the well-being of citizens. And yes, Europe must implement its single market to the fullest, something that Mario Draghi and Letta have been advocating for. Europe needs a 28th regime for firms. So if you go to Europe and you register in Germany, what you do is applicable in all 27 countries. Europe needs a capital market union. Europe needs free movement of skills across the Union and a unified energy system. All of this has to be done with optimal regulatory requirements, not with the regulatory capacity of Europe that it has today. So, yes, simplify. Absolutely, that's a very good point. You spoke about AI adding to global growth and taking it to pre-pandemic levels. I want to understand from you, you also spoke about fragmentation. Particularly for me, the question really that I want you to answer is: what's your sense about the long-term consequences of the changes in the tariff order, particularly from the United States which is the largest consuming economy in the world? In the medium to long term, Madam, do you think it's going to have a positive impact for growth or is it uncertain? What we have seen is very interesting. When the US announced tariffs, it was at a level much higher than what effectively the tariff level is today. So they moved in a direction of moderating the tariffs they proposed. But most importantly, how did the rest of the world react? The rest of the world said thank you, but no thank you. And not only do countries continue by and large to trade under the rules that have been established over decades, but there is an uptake of trade agreements, regional and cross-regional. India has its trade agreement with the UK, with the EU, and a tariff agreement with the US. India is also working on other trade agreements. That uptick shows that if globally we might be in a more difficult place to have uniform rules, countries would respond by regional and subregional integration. Here in South Asia there is tremendous potential to deepen trade integration within the subregion that for sure India can lead and benefit from. So my projection is the following: we are in a multipolar world. This is a reality. And therefore it would be more complex to have trade relations established. There would be more bilateral and plurilateral agreements. But the world would continue to trade. Supply chains will continue to cross borders. Why? Because we are integrated. We cannot pull apart. There is no going back to some place where everybody relies only on its own capacity. And if you go back in history, this is what we have been doing for thousands and thousands and thousands of years: trading. Trade is like water: you put an obstacle, and it goes around it. As we wind down this conversation, I want to ask you, is that your biggest worry? That with the new multipolar arrangements and the new evolving trade order, there will be many countries that will be left behind? The global trade order as it existed lifted many countries out of poverty, it created jobs, and it created a global supply chain. Do you worry that that is going to suffer? My biggest worry is that we are in a more shock-prone world. We have seen the pandemic, the war in Ukraine, the inflation spike, and high interest rates, one after another after another. Climate shocks are hitting many countries dramatically. This will continue. And what has happened over the last couple of years is twofold. One, many countries have exhausted their fiscal capacity to respond to shocks. They borrowed to help their businesses and their people and now the level of debt is high, and the level of growth is disappointing. When the next shock comes, rich countries are not going to be there to pour tons of money with the positive spillover for the rest of the world. It's just not possible. So this is my first worry: we exhausted buffers and we have to rebuild them. My second worry is that when a shock hits, what you said is going to be a tragedy in the making. The smaller, poorer countries—actually big poor countries too—they will be most dramatically impacted. And the world has weakened its collective capacity of solidarity in a moment of shock. I very much welcome the emerging market economies like India and like Brazil that are saying we have to nurture solidarity. They say it because it is the right thing to do, but also because in an unstable world there are risks for everyone. It is a matter of economic security not to let countries fall behind. I feel privileged as the head of the IMF because our job is to come to the rescue of countries in trouble. Right now we have 50 programs. One is right next to you in Sri Lanka. Why so many? Because so many countries are experiencing difficulty and because official development assistance has gone down. Yes, I'm proud we help countries, but I worry that it should not be only the IMF to be there for them. Finally, a message. India is a bright spot globally in terms of its own domestic growth, 7% plus give or take a few. But is the weakening climate consensus a worry for countries like India? It is a worry for everybody, everybody who cares about the future of our children and grandchildren, because the risks of climate change are real and they are already materializing in many places. India has many parts of the country that are vulnerable to climate shocks. But I also would say this is an opportunity for India, an opportunity for leadership. The International Solar Alliance is one example. It shows how India can step up and make sure that for its own people and for the betterment of the world, it is taking that leadership role. I see a lot in this growth boost in India that is great for the world. One, India contributes significantly to global growth, but also by being a dynamic innovation-driven economy, it sheds light for other countries to follow. And I see very often excitement that other countries can follow in your footsteps. Absolutely. Madam managing director, you've taken time to answer all these questions and give us a sense of how we are placed both at the macro level and at this moment in the age of AI. I thank you very much for your time. With that, it's a wrap on this conversation. If you've been watching, thank you very much for watching. Trump speaks after Supreme Court strikes down tariffs — 2/20/2026

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·45:06 ·157.9K views · 2026-02-20
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
President Trump condemned the Supreme Court’s ruling against his use of the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) for tariffs but announced he will immediately pivot to alternative legal authorities—including a new 10% global tariff—to ...
TL;DR President Trump condemned the Supreme Court’s ruling against his use of the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) for tariffs but announced he will immediately pivot to alternative legal authorities—including a new 10% global tariff—to maintain and expand his...

TL;DR

President Trump condemned the Supreme Court’s ruling against his use of the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) for tariffs but announced he will immediately pivot to alternative legal authorities—including a new 10% global tariff—to maintain and expand his protectionist trade agenda.

Response to Supreme Court Ruling

  • Legal Criticism: President Trump characterized the ruling as "deeply disappointing" and "incorrect," specifically criticizing Justices Gorsuch and Barrett while praising the dissents of Thomas, Alito, and Kavanaugh.
  • The "Fee" Paradox: Trump highlighted what he viewed as a logical inconsistency in the Court’s decision.
      • Authority to Embargo: The President noted that while the Court affirmed his right to "block, embargo, or restrict" trade entirely under IEEPA, it ruled he cannot charge a "licensing fee" or tariff.
      • Presidential Defiance: He argued that the power to "destroy a country" via embargo should logically include the lesser power to charge a fee, calling the restriction "ridiculous."
  • Allegations of Influence: Trump suggested the Court was swayed by "foreign interests," "political correctness," and a fear of "loud" radical-left movements.

Immediate Trade Policy Pivot

  • New 10% Global Tariff: Effective within three days, Trump announced a 10% across-the-board global tariff under Section 122 of the Trade Act of 1974.
  • Utilization of Alternative Statutes: Citing Justice Kavanaugh’s dissent, Trump stated that the ruling actually provides "certainty" by pointing to more "legally durable" authorities:
      • Trade Expansion Act of 1962 (Section 232): National security tariffs already in place will remain.
      • Trade Act of 1974 (Sections 201 and 301): New investigations are being initiated to target unfair trading practices.
      • Tariff Act of 1930 (Section 338): Identified as another tool for future implementation.
  • Status of Collected Revenue: Regarding the approximately $175 billion in tariff revenue currently in limbo, Trump declined to commit to refunds, stating the issue would likely be litigated for years.

Economic and National Security Claims

  • Market Milestones: Trump credited his trade policies for the Dow Jones Industrial Average breaking 50,000 and the S&P 500 surpassing 7,000 within one year of his election.
  • Domestic Manufacturing:
      • Steel Industry: Trump shared anecdotes from Georgia steel plants, claiming his tariffs rescued businesses from working "one hour a week" to running "24/7" shifts.
      • Tech and Chips: He claimed credit for "saving" Intel and forcing Taiwanese chip manufacturers to build factories in Arizona and Texas to avoid tariffs.
  • National Security and Fentanyl: The President claimed that using tariffs as penalties against specific countries has reduced fentanyl inflow by 30%.
  • Conflict Resolution: Trump asserted that the threat of 200% tariffs was the primary tool he used to "settle" eight international conflicts, including tensions between India and Pakistan.

Geopolitical Outlook and Foreign Relations

  • European Criticism: Trump described Europe as "soft" and "unrecognizable" due to environmental regulations and immigration policies, warning that they are "getting killed" on energy costs.
  • Relations with India and China:
      • India: Trump maintained that his relationship with Prime Minister Modi remains strong despite a "flip" in trade policy where India now pays tariffs to the U.S.
      • China: He noted that President Xi "respects our country now" due to the aggressive 20% penalty tariffs.
  • Iran: Trump issued a stern warning to Iranian leaders regarding human rights abuses, claiming his administration halted the scheduled execution of 837 people by threatening immediate military strikes.
"I am allowed to destroy the country [with an embargo], but I can’t charge them a little fee... It’s incorrect, their decision is incorrect, but it doesn’t matter because we have very powerful alternatives that have been approved by this decision." — President Donald Trump
"The decision might not substantially constrain a president's ability to order tariffs going forward... numerous other federal statutes authorize the president to impose tariffs." — Justice Brett Kavanaugh (quoted by Trump)
"We have a lot of tools out there... we're going to keep continuity in the program and we're going to keep addressing this so that the deficit can keep going down." — Jameson (Administration Official)
Wow, it's a lot of people. That's a new record. We set a record every time. Well, thank you very much for being here. The Supreme Court's ruling on tariffs is deeply disappointing, and I'm ashamed of certain members of the court—absolutely ashamed for not having the courage to do what's right for our country. I'd like to thank and congratulate Justices Thomas, Alito, and Kavanaugh for their strength and wisdom and love of our country, which is right now very proud of those justices. When you read the dissenting opinions, there's no way that anyone can argue against them; there's no way. Foreign countries that have been ripping us off for years are ecstatic; they're so happy. And they're dancing in the streets, but they won't be dancing for long, that I can assure you. The Democrats on the court are thrilled, but they will automatically vote no. They're an automatic no, just like in Congress; they're an automatic no. They're against anything that makes America strong, healthy, and great again. They also are frankly a disgrace to our nation, those justices. They're an automatic no, no matter how good a case you have, it's a no. But you can't knock their loyalty; it's one thing you can do with some of our people. Others think they're being politically correct, which has happened before far too often with certain members of this court, and it's happened so often with this court. What a shame, having to do with voting in particular, when in fact they're just being fools and lapdogs for the RINOs and the radical left Democrats. And, not that this should have anything at all to do with it, they're very unpatriotic and disloyal to our Constitution. It's my opinion that the court has been swayed by foreign interests and a political movement that is far smaller than people would ever think. It's a small movement. I won by millions of votes, we won in a landslide—with all the cheating that went on, there was a lot of it, but we still won in a landslide, too big to rig. But these people are obnoxious, ignorant, and loud. They're very loud. And I think certain justices are afraid of that. They don't want to do the right thing; they're afraid of it. This was an important case to me, more as a symbol of economic national security and also I would say just for our country itself, so important because we're doing so well as a country. We've never done so well. The good news is that there are methods, practices, statutes, and authorities, as recognized by the entire court in this terrible decision, and also as recognized by Congress, which they refer to, that are even stronger than the IEEPA tariffs available to me as President of the United States. And in actuality, I was very modest in my ask of other countries and businesses because I wanted to be very well-behaved. I didn't want to do anything that would affect the decision of the court because I understand the court; I understand how they are very easily swayed. I wanted to be a good boy. I have very effectively utilized tariffs over the past year to make America great again. Our stock market has just recently broken 50,000 on the Dow, and simultaneously and even more amazingly broken 7,000 on the S&P. Two numbers that everybody thought upon our landslide election victory could not be attained. Think of that—nobody thought it was possible to do it within four years, and we did it in one year. They said you'll never be able. You go back and you read the geniuses, read their statements. All of the Nobel Prize winners in economics, they said no you couldn't do it in four years. Well, we didn't do it in four years; we did it in one year. We broke every record in the book, and we're continuing to do so. Tariffs have likewise been used to end five of the eight wars that I settled. I settled eight wars whether you like it or not, including India and Pakistan—big ones, nuclear, could have been nuclear. The Prime Minister of Pakistan said yesterday at the great meeting that we had, the peace board, he said yesterday that President Trump could have saved 35 million lives by getting us to stop fighting. They were getting ready to do some bad things. But they've given us great national security, these tariffs have. And together with our strong borders, they reduced fentanyl coming into our country by 30% when I use them as a penalty against countries illegally sending this poison into our country to poison our youth. All of those tariffs remain. All of them remain. I don't know if you know that or not, but all of them remain. We're still getting them, and we will after the decision. I guess there's nobody left to appeal to, but again those three people—such respect. I've had a lot of respect for them anyway, but such great respect. But other alternatives will now be used to replace the ones that the court incorrectly rejected. We have alternatives, great alternatives. It could be more money; we'll take in more money and we'll be a lot stronger for it. We're taking in hundreds of billions of dollars, and we'll continue to do so. To show you how ridiculous the opinion is, however, the court said that I'm not allowed to charge even one dollar. I can't charge one dollar. Can't charge a dollar. I would have used one penny, but we don't make the pennies anymore—we save money. I can't charge one dollar to any country under IEEPA. Not one dollar. I assume to protect other countries. This must have been done to protect those other countries, certainly not the United States of America, which they should be interested in protecting. That's what they're supposed to be protecting. But I am allowed to cut off any and all trade or business with that same country. In other words, I can destroy the trade; I can destroy the country. I'm even allowed to impose a foreign country-destroying embargo. I can embargo, I can do anything I want, but I can't charge one dollar. Because that's not what it says, and that's not the way it even reads. I can do anything I want to do to them, but I can't charge any money. So I'm allowed to destroy the country but I can't charge them a little fee. I could give them a little two-cent fee, but I cannot charge under any circumstances. I cannot charge them anything. Think of that—how ridiculous is that? I'm allowed to embargo them, I'm allowed to tell them you can't do business in the United States anymore, we want you out of here. But if I want to charge them 10 dollars, I can't do that. It's incorrect. Their decision is incorrect, but it doesn't matter because we have very powerful alternatives that have been approved by this decision. You know they've been approved by the decision for those that thought they had us. And they're saying that I have the absolute right to license, but not the right to charge a licensing fee. So think of that. I have the right to license—it's a very powerful word in many ways. License is more powerful than tariffs; in fact, I was thinking about using it. But they came up with the idea that I can license just like the people that were opposing me told them to do, but not the right to charge a license fee. Think of that. Whoever heard of such a thing? What license has ever been issued without the right to charge a fee? You get a license, you charge a fee—it's automatic. But not with this court. But now the court has given me the unquestioned right to ban all sorts of things from coming into our country, to destroy foreign countries. It is a much more powerful right than many people ever thought we even had, but not the right to charge a fee. How crazy is that? Our country is the hottest country anywhere in the world right now, and it was a dead country one and a half years ago under an incompetent president. But now I'm going to go in a different direction, probably the direction that I should have gone the first time. But I read the language—I'm very good at reading language and it read our way 100%. But now I'll go the way I could have gone originally, which is even stronger than our original choice. As Justice Kavanaugh—whose stock has gone so high, you'll have to see, I'm so proud of him—wrote in his dissent: "Although I firmly disagree with the court's holding today, the decision might not substantially constrain a president's ability to order tariffs going forward." So think of that. The decision might not substantially constrain, and it doesn't. He's right. In fact, I can charge much more than I was charging. "That's because numerous other federal statutes," which is so true, "authorize the president to impose tariffs and might justify most if not all of the tariffs issued in this case." Even more tariffs actually. All clear. But it's a little bit longer process. I thought I'd make things simple but they didn't let us do that. In actuality, while I am sure that they did not mean to do so, the Supreme Court's decision today made a president's ability to both regulate trade and impose tariffs more powerful and more crystal clear rather than less. I don't think they meant that; I'm sure they didn't. It's terrible. And to think that the Democrats who oppose this only because they want to go the opposite way. There will no longer be any doubt, and the income coming in and the protection of our companies and country will actually increase because of this decision. I don't think the court meant that, but it's the way it is. Based on long-standing law and hundreds of victories—and even as I pointed out before, even thousands of victories over the years to the contrary—the Supreme Court did not overrule tariffs. They merely overruled a particular use of IEEPA tariffs. And essentially it's the use to get a fee. I can do anything I want with IEEPA, anything. I just can't charge anybody for it. The ability to block, embargo, restrict, license, or impose any other condition on a foreign country's ability to conduct trade with the United States under IEEPA has been fully confirmed by this decision. So now there's no doubt because, you know, there were a lot of questions about tariffs because no president was smart enough to use them to protect our country from those countries and businesses that were ripping us off. You took a look at the deficits that we had with some of these countries; it was disgraceful what they got away with for many, many decades. But now we know because this decision affirms all those things that some people weren't sure about in order to protect. And it says so, in order to protect our country a president can actually charge more tariffs than I was charging in the past period of a year under the various tariff authorities. So we can use other of the statutes, other of the tariff authorities, which have also been confirmed and are fully allowed. Therefore, effective immediately, all national security tariffs under Section 232 and existing Section 301 tariffs remain in place, fully in place and in full force and effect. Today I will sign an order to impose a 10% global tariff under Section 122 over and above our normal tariffs already being charged. And we're also initiating several Section 301 and other investigations to protect our country from unfair trading practices of other countries and companies. Thank you for your attention to this matter and I say quite simply what I've said for a long time: Make America Great Again. And interestingly we've already made it great so I don't have to use that, but I don't think we'll ever give up on MAGA. MAGA is always gonna be with us. If you have a few questions you can let us know, but just to end—so we're going forward, we will be able to take in more money and there'll no longer be doubt because there was always doubt. I know the people that brought the lawsuit and, you know, they're sleazebags—major sleazebags. But I know them and they're foreign country centric. AI Summit 2026 LIVE: Google CEO Sundar Pichai Speaks at India AI Summit | N18L

Firstpost(9.3M) ·45:30 ·1.5K views · 2026-02-20
Sundar Pichai (CEO, Google)

TL;DR

Google CEO Sundar Pichai announced a "full-stack commitment" to India’s AI future, centered on a $15 billion infrastructure investment, the goal of skilling 10 million people, and the deployment of localized AI products for healthcare, agriculture, and search.

AI-Powered Product Innovation and Social Impact

  • Developer Ecosystem: Google has contributed 22 Gemma models to "AI Kosh" to empower India’s developer community.
  • Public Sector Collaboration: Google is working with the Indian government on high-impact AI applications, including:
      • Agriculture: Delivering timely monsoon forecasts to farmers.
      • Healthcare: AI-driven screening for diabetic retinopathy.
      • Accessibility: Making information available in more local languages.
  • Consumer Adoption: India represents one of the world's highest adoption rates for voice and visual search.
      • Gemini App: Now available in 10 Indian languages.
      • Security: Scam detection features within "Circle to Search" and "Lens" see higher usage in India than anywhere else globally.

Workforce Development and Skilling

  • AI Skill House: A massive initiative designed to equip 10 million Indian leaders and professionals with AI tools to drive global progress.
  • Google AI Certificate: Launched in partnership with Wadhwani AI, this program targets students and early-career professionals to bridge the digital talent gap.

Digital Infrastructure and Connectivity

  • Vizag AI Hub: A central component of Google’s $15 billion infrastructure investment, this hub will house gigawatt-scale compute capacity to support businesses across the country.
  • India-America Connect Initiative: A major project to deliver new subsea cable routes connecting the U.S., India, and the Southern Hemisphere.
      • Digital Trade Routes: These cables serve as a "literal bridge" to expand trade and data flow between the two nations.
  • Supply Chain Security: Emphasis on the "FACT Silicon" initiative to ensure safe, secure, and stable cross-border supply chains for critical tech components.

The US-India Strategic Partnership

  • Innovation Export: Pichai highlighted that innovations starting in India, such as Google Pay, are now improving products globally.
  • Bilateral Tech Foundation: The combination of recent trade agreements and infrastructure projects creates a long-term foundation for a robust US-India tech partnership.
  • Unified Operations: Google maintains seamless teams across both countries to work on core global initiatives.
"I believe India is going to have an extraordinary trajectory with AI, and we are supporting with a full-stack commitment including products, skilling, and infrastructure." — Sundar Pichai, CEO of Google
"The India-America Connect initiative... will significantly expand the digital trade routes and serve as a literal bridge between our two countries." — Sundar Pichai, CEO of Google
"None of this would be possible without stable supply chains built on a foundation of shared trust." — Sundar Pichai, CEO of Google
Including products, skilling, and infrastructure. First products: we are working on building AI products and solutions for Indian consumers and businesses. To empower India's incredible developer community, we've already contributed 22 Gemma models to AI Kosh. And we are working closely with the government to bring AI applications with real-world impact, be it through delivering timely monsoon forecasts to farmers, helping healthcare workers screen for diseases like diabetic retinopathy, or making information and services accessible in more languages. Our commitment extends to reimagining the products people use every day. As one example, AI is changing the way people use search. Indian users are amongst the highest adopters of voice and visual search globally. Our scam detection features with Circle to Search and Lens are used in India more than anywhere else. The Gemini app is growing rapidly across the world, and it's available in 10 languages spoken in India. And YouTube supports a vibrant ecosystem of Indian content creators sharing music, arts, and culture with the world. Second, skilling. Through the AI Skill House, we are working to equip 10 million future Indian leaders with the tools to drive global progress. We're also partnering with Wadhwani AI to reach students and early career professionals with our Google AI certificate, which we announced earlier this week. Third, infrastructure. Last year, we announced a 15 billion dollar investment in Indian infrastructure, with the AI Hub in Vizag at the center. This hub will house gigawatt-scale compute. When finished, it'll bring jobs and the benefits of cutting-edge AI to people and businesses across India. Building on this, we recently announced the India-America Connect initiative, which will deliver new subsea cable routes to connect the US, India, and multiple locations across the Southern Hemisphere. Combined with our existing cable systems, this initiative will significantly expand the digital trade routes and serve as a literal bridge between our two countries. Of course, none of this would be possible without stable supply chains built on a foundation of shared trust. Products, subsea cables, and AI hubs are all dependent on a complex flow of goods and components across borders. FACT Silicon focuses on making sure that these supply chains are safe and secure and encourages greater commercial partnerships across key technologies. So let me congratulate the US and India on this historic moment. Alongside the recent trade agreement, this will lay a strong foundation for a robust US-India tech partnership for many years to come. Thank you. Are available to everyone and everywhere. The US-India partnership has a critical role to play. Google is proud to serve as a connection point between them, both figuratively and literally. More on this later. We have teams across both countries working seamlessly together on some of our most important initiatives. Innovations that start in India, like Google Pay, are making products better for people all over the world. I believe India is going to have an extraordinary trajectory with AI, and we are supporting with a full-stack commitment including products, skilling, and infrastructure. First products: we are working on building AI products and solutions for Indian consumers and businesses. To empower India's incredible developer community, we've already contributed 22 Gemma models to AI Kosh. And we are working closely with the government to bring AI applications with real-world impact, be it through delivering timely monsoon forecasts to farmers, helping healthcare workers screen for diseases like diabetic retinopathy, or making information and services accessible in more languages. Our commitment extends to reimagining the products people use every day. As one example, AI is changing the way people use search. Indian users are amongst the highest adopters of voice and visual search globally. Our scam detection features with Circle to Search and Lens are used in India more than anywhere else. The Gemini app is growing rapidly across the world, and it's available in 10 languages spoken in India. And YouTube supports a vibrant ecosystem of Indian content creators sharing music, arts, and culture with the world. Second, skilling. Through the AI Skill House, we are working to equip 10 million future Indian leaders with the tools to drive global progress. We're also partnering with Wadhwani AI to reach students and early career professionals with our Google AI certificate, which we announced earlier this week. Third, infrastructure. Last year, we announced a 15 billion dollar investment in Indian infrastructure, with the AI Hub in Vizag at the center. This hub will house gigawatt-scale compute. When finished, it'll bring jobs and the benefits of cutting-edge AI to people and businesses across India. Building on this, we recently announced the India-America Connect initiative, which will deliver new subsea cable routes to connect the US, India, and multiple locations across the Southern Hemisphere. Combined with our existing cable systems, this initiative will significantly expand the digital trade routes and serve as a literal bridge between our two countries. Of course, none of this would be possible without stable supply chains built on a foundation of shared trust.
