When you think about the entire value chain that got created, for example, for the mobile industry, there's an enormous amount of value in things like OSs and application stores. And that becomes like the platform when you're going to develop an application that you're going to do different things into. An agent now understands human intentions because, you know, you just need to tell him what you want, or he is intelligence is shifting from something we utilize to something that's simply all around us everywhere, all the time going to see what you see and and and make a decision for you, assuming you authorize it. When that happens, that's where the value is because then the agent is free. It can go to the internet and do things, it can go to your phone and do things, and you're no longer bound by constructs of your hardware or OS and the application. So as a result, we expect that AI is going to have a fundamental shift in the mobile industry where the agent is going to be at the very center. And as the agent is at the very center, everything surrounds the agent. You can access the agent from your mobile phone, but you can also access the agent from your glasses or for a pendant or for anything that you wear. And I think we're going to look at the mobile ecosystem right now not only as a single device experience but you're going to connect to agents across multiple types of devices. And I think this is incredibly exciting. And that's not only unique to what you're going to see in consumers; that's going to happen also with things, you know, because you now can now also create AI that's going to get trained on different things—on physical signals like physical AI, on sensor data—and you're going to deploy that in every computer. So what's exciting about AI is it's going to very quickly evolve from something where you go to a browser and you ask a question. And I think, as my colleague from Cisco said, it's got trained on all the publicly available data on the internet. You're now going to go to a different type of AI experience that's going to be the fundamental software that is going to run in all the devices around us and how you're going to have interaction on those devices. So I also want to basically, as we think about this future, I just want to give you an example. What we saw across the industry is workloads or use cases have shifted. Devices didn't go anywhere, but their workload shifted. We used to do a lot of things in the early days of the internet on your laptop. And for example, e-commerce: you would do it on your laptop. Now most of the e-commerce in the world is done on a phone. Tomorrow—or it could be as early as within the end of this year as you start to see the proliferation of glasses—if you have a glass that has agents, it's connected to the internet and has a camera, the glass sees what you see. You can just look at something and say, "I'd like to buy this." What is it? "Can you check this, for example, check this on Flipkart, and just buy it for me? I'd like to buy this." With the integration of payment systems, you get a bill and say, "Pay this, notify me when I'm done," and so forth. So I think we're going to see this fundamental change of devices. But that's also going to be true about the revolution that's happening on robotics and the revolution that is actually happening on industrial. So that's an incredible opportunity, and we have been incredibly focused as a company to basically drive that future of computing. There's also a big debate, which I believe is the wrong way to look into it, which is about cloud and edge. There's a lot of debate about, "Oh, this is going to be running on the cloud," or "This is going to be running on the edge." And it actually does not matter. I want to think about your device today. Your smartphone today has an incredible amount of processing power, and there are a number of different things that run in our smartphone. If you put it on airplane mode, you probably don't use it. You just put it back and wait until you get connectivity again. It's the most cloud-connected device because those things work as one. It is, and you're going to have now intelligence that's going to be incredibly distributed across the cloud, across the near edge, the network in itself, and on-device. And it's all going to work seamlessly. There are going to be things that you're going to be able to do on the device because they require an instant response, or it requires unique context, requiring unique information that is relevant to you. Something else is going to be done on the cloud, and they're both going to be growing and transforming how we think about computers. So I'd like to provide a simple description. Let's say we are all using agents, and you're going to pick the agent that you like. For the agent to be useful, it needs to be fast and it needs to be relevant for you. Let's go back to the example I provided on the glasses. And you have those smart glasses and you're walking around and you have a camera, then all of a sudden you see somebody and you ask this glass, like it's a friend next to you, "Who is this person?" And you want to get a response. "This is so and so." Or you're going to say, "Can you translate this for me? What is this? Can you pay this for me?" You want this thing to be seamless, with no friction. So certain things are going to be done on your device and other things are going to be on the cloud; it's going to be completely transparent to you. But the interesting thing is those agents, for them to be very useful, they need to be contextually aware of what is relevant to you. So over time, the agent that I'm going to be using and the agent that you're going to be using—they need to be relevant to me. So you're going to have a lot of things that are going to be processed and understood about you. So much so that I believe that in the endgame—I think it was said in the prior presentation from Cisco—all this available data that is publicly on the internet that you train models with is a fraction of the data that is going to be generated. If you have, for example, a glass with a camera that sees everything that you see, it tries to annotate that image, get information about the image and the context, reads what you read, and so forth. That is an incredible amount of data, and that's going to be providing a lot of important context for those models to be relevant to you. That is the future, and it's an incredible transformation. It's going to transform every industry. No industry is immune to this, and I think what we are doing as Qualcomm is really creating the future hardware and software that will help enable this future across all the devices. We were a very unique semiconductor company. I think we're probably one of the few companies that can be working on chips from sub-milliwatts for a smart earbud that you're going to wear, all the way to now 2,000 watts for a chip on the data center. But I think that's the incredible future: that AI is going to transform every single computer and the agents are going to be at the center of the experience. It's going to replace a lot of the OSs and application definitions we see today. And I think that that is the new future of technology, including the future of mobility. And that's why I am incredibly excited about this. And with that, I want to talk about something that is happening which is about the next generation of wireless technologies. I would like to provide an example from the past. When you think about telecom networks—and I think we're probably one of the, you know, American telecom companies that are really focused on the evolution of cellular technology—when you think about the evolution of this sector, it all started with providing a telephone. I think for all of us, it was an incredible thing. You have a twisted copper pair that gets to your home, you pick up, you get a dial tone, you dial, and eventually, you could dial anybody in the world with a telephone. Even how cellular started was about making sure all of us had the ability to carry a telephone. That was 2G, where you can call everyone. That's different today. Now you have a very high-performance broadband network for data. Voice is just one application among many applications that you do at the network. It fundamentally changed the nature of the infrastructure; the equipment was different, the use cases were different. We're heading to the next big transformation of the telecom sector. 6G is going to provide an evolution of connectivity: faster speed, lower latency, higher coverage. But that's not the story. That's just a piece of the story. This just continues to improve the connectivity. The bigger part of 6G is AI. Like I said before, it is now going to come to the telecom network. And that becomes a large-scale AI network that is processing and getting trained on all of the signals that happen at the network and providing new capabilities. One of the biggest features of 6G is that the network is a sensing network at scale. I'm going to give an example. The network not only will provide connectivity between your device and the internet but will sense everything that's around you. It will use techniques that you see today in autonomous driving cars, like radars as an example, to detect your environment. And it's going to provide a map of everything that is happening at scale. And you're going to have completely different types of services for different industries. It will provide context for your agents, which is very important, and the network will have that role. It will provide traffic management systems in some of the use cases that are going to be part of full self-driving cars. It will do drone detection and manage the traffic control of the economy that's going to be aerial in the wide area network, and much more, because AI is also going to the network. It's going to be one of the biggest transitions I think we've had, as big as going from voice to data, and it's all part of this future of AI. And I just want now to make another parallel to the presentation from my colleague from Cisco. It puts a fine point on the network that needs to be built, the capability of the infrastructure, the security, and the trust. But that is an incredible future with technology. And as I get to the end of the presentation, I want to highlight that India has an incredible opportunity with this transformation. We have seen that those big shifts in technology create opportunity, change players, and change the role of different countries as they provide a global scale for the technology. And that's an incredible opportunity for India. I look at what happened in mobile in India, and one of the largest data consumption rates per user in mobile devices in the world is in India. The whole internet is mobile. When you think about the potential of all those things that I just discussed—about how AI is going to change everything, creating new devices, new experiences, new services—that becomes a massive opportunity. And when I look at the ambitions that were set by the AI summit, I'm going to provide just some examples. Those are just examples. It can be much broader, but I just want to connect with some of the ambitions of the summit. There is a process of jumping into large-scale industrialization. India is becoming a global manufacturing hub as well, and with AI, you go from the very beginning with smart manufacturing and automation. The incredible change that is happening in this sector is enabled by those technologies. Same thing with smart cities and the ability to continue to evolve the infrastructure. There is the ability to use AI to increase the scale, the reach, and the access for healthcare. Consider how you change education. Those are incredibly powerful learning tools—the ability to actually use some of those technologies to empower people with information. And you're going to have an ongoing learning experience. Think about those agents that are with you all the time, answering questions, telling you how to do things, especially when you think in the context, for example, of those new devices such as smart glasses. And it can fundamentally change industries, for example, such as agriculture. And these are just a few examples of the potential of connecting this technology with everything I think that is going on in India. It's an incredible and exciting future enabled by AI, and it's really going to be about meeting the ambition of democratizing this technology for everyone and actually having an important role in increasing the global welfare. And you know, as a company that has always been focused on enabling our partners in other industries to innovate, I think the history of Qualcomm is that we never believe it is the job of one company to be responsible for all the innovation. It's really to enable many industries and partners. We're incredibly excited to play a very small part in this mission. Thank you very much for the opportunity to talk to all of you, and enjoy the rest of the summit. Thank you.

How AI Is Accelerating The Race Between Hackers And Corporate Security Teams

Forbes(2.1M) ·35:04 ·513 views · 2026-02-20
Brian Dye (CEO, Corelight)
> "It used to be that the time from a vulnerability to an exploit was about three weeks.
TL;DR: Corelight provides deep network visibility to protect elite critical infrastructure from increasingly sophisticated cyber threats, specifically addressing the AI-driven "acceleration gap" where hackers can now weaponize software vulnerabilities in hours rather than weeks....

TL;DR: Corelight provides deep network visibility to protect elite critical infrastructure from increasingly sophisticated cyber threats, specifically addressing the AI-driven "acceleration gap" where hackers can now weaponize software vulnerabilities in hours rather than weeks.

The Open-Source Foundation: From Zeke to Enterprise

  • Academic Roots: The company originated as an open-source project named "Zeke" at the Lawrence Berkeley National Lab. It was funded by the National Science Foundation for 15 years before transitioning into a commercial entity to support its community of 300,000 security professionals.
  • Visibility Strategy: CEO Brian Dye uses an "egg" analogy to describe security—while most companies focus on the "shell" (prevention), Corelight provides visibility inside the "yolk" to find attackers who have already breached the perimeter.
  • Customer Profile: Approximately 70% of Corelight’s business serves high-stakes sectors, including:
      • The "Five Eyes" intelligence partners and NATO allies.
      • U.S. Departments of Defense, Energy, and Justice.
      • Major critical infrastructure providers, such as global banks and energy companies.

AI and the Shrinking Vulnerability Window

  • Speed of Exploitation: AI has dramatically accelerated the race between attackers and defenders.
      • The Metric: Historically, the time from a software vulnerability being discovered to an exploit being launched was roughly three weeks. In the current AI-driven environment, that window has shrunk to approximately three hours.
      • Volume: One customer reported being attacked 23,000 times in the 72-hour window it took to patch a single vulnerability.
  • The Defender’s Advantage: Corelight’s open-source nature means Large Language Models (LLMs) like ChatGPT, Llama, and Gemini are already trained on decades of Zeke documentation and community data. This allows defenders to use AI out of the box to automate investigations and responses without custom training.
  • Automation Requirements: To match attacker speed, elite organizations are now automating the response to 95% of inbound threats with a 15-second Service Level Agreement (SLA).

Detection and Ransomware Mitigation

  • Living off the Land: Attackers increasingly use a company’s own legitimate tools to remain stealthy. Corelight specializes in detecting this anomalous behavior and tracing "breadcrumbs" to identify the full path of an intrusion.
  • Calling the Ransomware Bluff: Dye cited a case study where a customer faced a $10 million ransomware demand.
      • The attacker claimed to have stolen massive amounts of sensitive data.
      • Corelight’s data proved the attacker only accessed 10% of what they claimed.
      • This high-fidelity insight allowed the board to refuse the ransom payment with confidence.
  • The "Carbon-Based" Vulnerability: Despite technical advances, social engineering remains the hardest vulnerability to "patch," as AI-driven audio and video spoofs make human employees the weakest link in the chain.

Leadership and Scaling Insights

  • Inverting the Spending Ratio: While typical companies spend a fraction of their IT budget on security, Corelight spends four times more on cybersecurity than on its internal IT operations.
  • Strategic Delegation: Dye noted the transition from a $2M startup to a $100M+ enterprise required a "bounce" back to delegation. At the startup phase, the CEO is "doing" (e.g., prioritizing engineering tickets); at scale, the CEO must empower the team to make decisions without them rolling up to the top.
  • Executive Hiring Lessons: Dye admitted that attempting to hire four direct reports simultaneously was a mistake. He recommends "serializing" executive hires to ensure deep cultural fit and focus rather than parallel-pathing multiple high-stakes recruitments.

Notable Quotes

"It used to be that the time from a vulnerability to an exploit was about three weeks. Now it's about three hours. In the 72 hours it took [one customer] to patch that, they got attacked 23,000 times." — Brian Dye
"The difference between 'I think' and 'I know' is incredibly valuable in this space... it could be the difference between notifying a thousand people and notifying 20 or 30,000 people." — Brian Dye
"One of the great pieces of advice from one of our board members was 'I can raise more money, I can't raise more time.'... Buying yourself more time doesn't help you solve a mistake you can't see coming." — Brian Dye
"One of the hazards of our industry is there's way too many folks kind of beating their chest and talking about how they're perfect. This is the opposite of that. It's a really humble group serving incredibly elite organizations." — Brian Dye
Hi everyone. I'm Maggie McGrath, Senior Editor at Forbes. In 2025, more than 16 billion login credentials were stolen through cyber attacks according to cybernews.com. Our next guest is helping protect companies and their customers against these very attacks. He is Brian Dye, he's the CEO of Corelight. Brian, thanks so much for joining us. Maggie, thanks for having me. Looking forward to the conversation. So some folks may not have heard of Corelight. It is an open source company that has its roots in academia, but can you give us the 60-second elevator pitch? Of course. Corelight helps defend the world's most critical networks by ensuring attackers have no place to hide. And what we really do, if you think about defending an organization, right, think of it like an egg. You, you want to start at the shell, right, prevent the attacker from getting in. It turns up attackers have found ways through that shell in many cases. So then we really help give visibility to what's going on within the egg, right? Once that attacker's in there trying to move around, trying to get their target, whether that's ransomware where they're actually stealing data and trying to actually hold it for ransom, or whether it's a nation-state adversary, we actually help them really get that visibility so the attacker can't hide inside the network. How do you do that? And how does the open source roots of Corelight help you do that, if at all? The open source roots are actually a great foundational story because the company was never intended to be a company, right? The point, the entire open source project started as a postdoc who was working in the Lawrence Berkeley National Lab, a PhD postdoc. He was studying networks and how they behaved and so he wrote this little tool, this little utility to help him do his work. It turns up that "Can you tell me what's happening on my network?" is shockingly useful to a really, really wide range of people. Whether you're running an IT network, whether you're trying to secure an IT network, whether you're researching that network, everybody wants that data. So this little utility became its side project and then it started getting used across the national labs through the Department of Energy and then it moved into the Department of War and the high-end financials, even into the intelligence community, into U.S. allies and beyond. So it kind of had this life of its own and kind of got expanded, and it was being funded by the National Science Foundation for the better part of 15 years. And eventually, the National Science Foundation said, "This is great, it's really successful. We are not in the business of being an ongoing support mechanism for software projects. You all need to find an economic vehicle to be successful." And that actually forced the creation of the company because we needed an economic vehicle to support what is now a community of over 300,000 security professionals that rely on this tool and the data and the insight it generates on a day-to-day basis. So that was the nucleus of the company: taking this idea of, "Hey, everybody needs to know what's happening inside your network," but that with so many people that are using it, it needs an economic entity to make sure it's durable and helping all those folks—not just commercially, but the free users within the community as well. So you are not the founding CEO. You came in and took over for the founders. You have experience at McAfee and Symantec. What made you join a relative startup? It has its roots in maybe a time that makes the company mature enough that we can't call it a startup, but what made you join from established tech companies to a startup? Yeah, it's a great question, and it was definitely a startup at the time. We had 30 people, we had 2 million of revenue. That, that is the definition of a startup. One with a great open source community, but certainly still a startup. For me, it was simple. From a personal standpoint and a career standpoint, I had done larger-scale things, I had worked in established companies. But I live in Silicon Valley, and I hadn't done a technology startup in Silicon Valley. That's a bit like being in the Air Force and not being a pilot. Right? Go to where the action is. So let's go tackle that. And then when I looked at the ecosystem, there were 200 cyber startups within commuting distance of my house. Right? I live in the middle of the peninsula in Palo Alto, so if you just look San Jose to San Francisco at that time, there were over 200. And I wouldn't say I talked to every single one of them, but I talked to a pretty fair chunk and I knew enough about the industry to, to kind of analyze it. And what really drew me to Corelight was four things. I call them the four Ps. Number one, there was really clearly a problem, right? This idea of really advanced nation-state attackers being hard to detect with current technology. Organizations were starting to make investments in what are called threat hunting teams, right? A lot of security relies on the blinking red light. An alert went off, go investigate that alert. The idea behind threat hunting is, the alert didn't go off but there's somebody in there anyway. So can we go find them? Right? So we saw people betting with headcount, right? They were making an organizational investment. And when they did so, they were really relying on this open source technology. So there's this product-market fit proof, you know, of these 300,000 people in the open source community. But then beyond that, there was so much potential. There were all these adjacent technologies, adjacent tools that we knew we could get into, because if you're going to go bet on a small company, you don't want to bet on a one-trick pony, right? From a career standpoint, that's not entirely brilliant. I wanted to make sure that there was something that could grow into a really enduring, impactful company. You need enough adjacent markets, enough TAM to go and tackle that. And then the last one was just the people. And I give a lot of credit to Greg Bell, who was the founding CEO, and the founders of the open source project, Vern, Robin, and Seth, who all know their names. They had created a company that was so unlike other cybersecurity companies. Right? One of the hazards of our industry is there's way too many folks kind of beating their chest and talking about how they're perfect and how they've got everything sorted. This is kind of the opposite of that. It's a really humble group. We serve incredibly elite organizations, and we try to do so from that perspective. You mentioned incredibly elite organizations. Did I read that you work with a number of government agencies and the Department of Defense? Is that correct? Yeah, about 70% of our business is what I'll jokingly say where private companies aren't supposed to sell. So think about the biggest critical infrastructure providers in the world: banks, energy companies, things like that. Think about the intelligence community, the defense departments, the energy departments at U.S. and U.S. allies, right, between the U.S., what are called the Five Eyes, right, which are our Intel partners, NATO, and many of the U.S. allies kind of within NATO. Good chunk of those are actually customers. So it's really unusual. Most private cyber companies start in the mid-enterprise and work their way up. Corelight's the unusual company that started in the very large enterprise and gov, and we're working our way down. And did those large enterprises come from those 300,000 security professionals who were using the open source technology, or do you look at those as kind of independent sets? It was about a mix, actually. So it's about a 50-50 split in the history of the company. Right? The open source project is called Zeke, which is neither here nor there, but it makes the acronym easy. Half of our customers traditionally were aware of the open source, you know, hard to make it scale, hard to make it performant, they needed a commercial alternative. The other half we call zero to Zeke. They had never heard of it before, but once they realized that all the cool kids are using it, like "Oh wait, this is really powerful and here's why and these other defenders are using it," then they want to come onboard and do that too, and it's a lot easier to start with a commercial alternative than it is with the open source per se. Well, I ask because I heard you say in an interview that the scale and speed of these attacks and the sophistication of the attacks that we're seeing in late 2025, early 2026, are such that it's not just the most elite companies or organizations or governments in the world, it is everybody, all the way down to maybe small-medium enterprises. Is that your experience in talking to customers these days? It's absolutely what's happening. So if you think about what we as commoners are all getting from the advantages of all these GenAI tools, right, whether you use Gemini or OpenAI AI whatever, right? I was working on a keynote for the company's kickoff, coming up shortly. That keynote used to take me three weeks, now it took me four hours. Wonderful acceleration. Only problem is, the attackers are getting the same advantage. Right? It used to be, and we heard this in a customer advisory board just last month, it used to be that a new vulnerability, right, a flaw in software that attackers are using to exploit, the time from a vulnerability to an exploit, actually using it, used to be about three weeks. Now it's about three hours. So we had multiple customers that had the story of, "Oh, it used to be three weeks, now it's three hours." And by the way, in the 72 hours it took us to patch that, we got attacked 23,000 times. So they were having a person that was sitting there just at a console: stop attack, stop attack, stop attack, stop attack, for 72 hours literally trying to shut stuff down. And what's going on: the GenAI models are helping the attackers do these exploits faster and it's helping them fill their skill gaps. Right? What used to be these incredibly advanced attacks now get sedimented down and are easier to adopt by your kind of more run-of-the-mill attackers, right? Because they have career paths, by the way, just like the rest of us. So that's really what's happening. We need an attacker for this C-suite series and figuring out how do you go from baby attacker to CEO attacker. But you mentioned the red light going off, kind of as the warning. Where in the process of an attack does Corelight come in, or is the value proposition on the detection, is it on the fixing, patching the breach? Yeah, we're on the detection and response side. So if you think about protection, there's a lot of great technologies and great companies that are focusing on that problem because, going back to the egg analogy, you do want a firm shell. If you can't take out the broad-base swath of internet noise, which, as you said earlier, is in the billions range, that has to be fully automated, fully machine-driven, right? That's gotta be automatic. Once you're inside, there's a different class of behavior you're trying to detect. To use an example, there's a technique that's become a lot more common now called living off the land. Because as an attacker, you want to be as stealthy as you can once you got in. Gives you time to kind of figure out what's on the network, almost think of it like someone who's casing your house. Right? The thief that wants to case your house doesn't want you to know they're casing your house. Right? So they want to go in, be very quiet, be very stealthy. The best way to do that is to use the tools the company is already using. Because now you can't tell what's an attacker and what's just a legitimate employee. So we help detect that anomalous activity that's happening within the network. And then, once that attack happens, now you have to figure out the entire path. Because you're not just, think about again back to that housebreaking analogy, you're not just looking at your safe. Let's pretend we all have safes. Dated analogy, but hang with me. Let's pretend we had a safe. You don't want to just know how they broke into your safe, you want to know how they got into your house. You want that whole path that kind of led them there. And so we help trace all the breadcrumbs back. You know, to make that maybe a little more concrete, we had a customer about a year and a half ago that was under a ransomware attack. And it turns up there's no honor among thieves. The ransomware attacker claimed to have taken all this data, and that was why it was a threatening attack to the board and why they had this, you know, $10 million ransomware demand. Because we could actually tell them where the attacker did and did not go, that customer could prove that the attacker had only stolen about 10% of what they said they did. And that, yeah, that 10% actually wasn't that brand damaging, so it gave them the confidence, not just within the technical team but up to the board and the CEO, to say, "Hey, we're calling your bluff, we're not paying the ransom, we're walking away." So that difference between "I think" and "I know" is incredibly valuable in this space. So when I as a customer have gotten emails from retailers or restaurants or sites that have my personal data and they are reporting a breach but they say we know it's only 200,000 of you, or if they give any sort of disclosure about the the scope of the attack, is it likely that that data's coming from you? I don't want to over-attribute to us, but that is a big problem that we're trying to solve because if you think about someone who's been attacked and there's a bunch of SEC disclosure regulations and everything that goes with this, if they can't prove a smaller scope of disclosure, they have to default to a much bigger one. So if you think about how cybersecurity translates from the technical domain up into the leadership team and the board, it really is a brand damage and a disclosure scope problem. Because again that difference between "I think" and "I know" could be the difference between notifying a thousand people and notifying 20 or 30,000 people. And so it really is about kind of getting the smallest scope of disclosure and you just have to have the confidence of the ground truth data of what's actually happening behind that. Is there a common vulnerability you see among Corelight's customers, the people who come to you say, "Hey, I need your help"? I mean, we talked about the speed of the attacks, the sophistication of the attacks, but as you're looking at companies and organizations across the world, can you say, "Hey, everyone is really terrible with their firewalls," or is there a common symptom? We have such a privilege to serve really, really elite defense organizations. I would be doing them no service if I said any of them were weak or incapable. Because bear in mind these are folks with hundreds, sometimes even thousands of people in their security team. They're quite sophisticated. The biggest two problems that people wrestle with: one is the IT industry itself, right? The IT industry, especially in the last 18 months, has shipped too many products with their own vulnerabilities that then attackers use to bypass all the other security defenses. That's a really hard problem. So again, you're breaking through this shell of the egg and now you're in the yolk, right? Which is why they need our help. The second one, I hate to say it, is us. It's you and me. A common theme I hear from customers is the weakest part of their network is the carbon-based part. Right? And that would be the breathing people, us. Because we click on links we shouldn't. Right? That's a big way that attackers get in. And back to your point on AI earlier, they are absolutely using AI to get more effective at social engineering. It is really savvy and, and they can do so much effective research down to, you know, audio spoofs, video spoofs even, bypassing help desks. The human side of this is an incredibly hard to patch vulnerability. It's really hard. And this might never cross your desk, but on my social feeds I've seen over the past few weeks and months people talking about, "I got a call from my bank saying that there was a fraudulent transaction and the number matched what I thought was my bank's number, so I took the call." And then they kind of go down the path of answering the questions that the person on the other line is asking. And the savvy ones realize, "Hey, my bank would never actually ask me these questions," but some of these attackers on the consumer side are so sophisticated that some people get to the end of the call and only then have the "Oh my god" moment. Well, and as a quick yes-and, this is one of my favorite funny Corelight stories. I warn new employees that they will likely get what's called a smishing attack, right? A text message claiming to be from me, asking to approve a PO or get them gift cards. Usually within the first 30 days. That's how long it takes because folks go post their new jobs on LinkedIn, and the attackers are scraping LinkedIn for new job opportunities. And my favorite part is sometimes I will also get those smishing attacks, so I will get text messages claiming to be from myself. Well, at least you know that you didn't do that. Pretty straightforward to figure that one out. That's a good warning. So was there an employee who fell victim to one of those smishing and therefore you have to warn everyone? Oh, fortunately we do not fall victim to those. We take our anti-phishing training quite seriously and because we're a cyber provider you get pretty cyber-savvy folks. I'm not saying we're perfect, we're not, by the way. But no, it's one of those times where it's a good thing where employees see it, they ask me or they ask our security department or they ask our peers, and they very quickly are told, "Oh, of course that's not Brian." And, you know, we have an open enough culture that my cellphone's on the directory just like anyone else, so if you want to look it up and see if it's me, you have a pretty easy way to do that. But kind of on that note, I wonder, do you have like a 10-question security Q&A that is a screener for all potential employees? We don't rely on that as a screener. We rely on it as education once folks come onboard. Because if you think about the obligation that we have to our customers, right? If you're providing security software to some of the largest and most sophisticated defense organizations in the world, that have hundreds to thousands of people, we can't be the weak link in their chain. Right? That is just a company-ending risk for us. So we take our employee training really seriously. We take our ongoing kind of patching or updates really seriously. And, you know, I often look at customers and think about the staffing ratios they have. So for example, you can look at how savvy an organization is in their cyber defense by saying, "Hey, what percentage of revenue do you spend on IT? And what percentage of IT do you spend on security?" Pretty easy math. It turns up for us that number is inverted, right? So we actually spend four times more on cyber than we spend on the rest of IT. Right? That's the level of priority we give that. Cyber spending would be a quarter of IT, not the other way around. Should more companies be taking your model and inverting that? I'd be hard-pressed to make that recommendation especially to organizations at scale. Right? Because as you grow, the IT—like let's take a high-end bank, right, that's doing a lot of application development in-house. Right? Their IT organization in many cases looks like our engineering organization, right? Because they're developing the products that the bank is shipping to you and I as end customers. Right? So you kind of have to think about that ratio a little bit differently. It'd be hard-pressed to say your security organization should be bigger than your engineering organization, right? To use that ratio. So I think people do need to take it seriously. But I don't think in many cases what's holding folks back is less about the overall staffing ratio. It's more about the willingness and the culture of the company to really embrace this. And to give a quick sidebar if it's useful: we have a big university in the south of the U.S. that's a customer of ours that's not just a university, they actually act as a service provider to a bunch of the schools, the other schools, and even the educational system in that state. We call them MSSPs, managed security service providers. The biggest organizational win they had was to get the provost of the colleges to treat cyber as a priority to everyone else. They could not be anywhere near as effective, even though they have a very healthy staff and really good technology. They actually bring the undergrads into their cyber program; they're doing everything right. But even with that, if they didn't have the support of the provost to make cyber culturally important and actually part of the lines of business, right—the other schools within that university—they wouldn't be able to achieve the results they have. That's interesting. I want to get back to hiring and leadership questions, but before we do, before we started recording, you and I were talking about an experience that you had at a customer advisory board meeting in 2022 and it kind of goes back to the questions about how AI is changing the landscape. I heard you talk about this in another interview and it's so interesting. You were having this meeting with the customer advisory board and someone raises their hand and talks about how they're using ChatGPT in conjunction with Corelight, and it was kind of a meeting-stopping moment. Can you talk about how ChatGPT has changed your business or customers' use of it? Oh, I'd love to. And it's not just ChatGPT, it's all of the big foundation language models, right? So the story you're referring to was literally in March of 2022. So this is a few weeks after GPT-3.5 really hit the wire, kind of public awareness went through the roof, adoption was going through the roof. I hadn't even opened the thing at that point, right? So I can take zero credit for anything that's about to come out of my mouth here. So we're on this advisory board meeting, and again, high-end financial customer, very early adopter of technology. We were trying to talk about machine learning and advanced analytics and all the blinking red light stuff. And she stopped us and said, "I thought you all should know that my analysts are using GPT-3.5 to automatically drive security investigations using the insight Corelight provides." And as you said, that was a meeting-stopping moment because we're all like, "What did she just say? We need to learn more. This is awesome." And what happened is, and I've said this multiple times, I've never worked in a company with an open source business model before. And it turns out open source is the gift that keeps on giving. How did this work? The large language models are all trained on the public internet. We're open source. There is almost 30 years of not just open source product documentation and Reddit threads and, you know, community forums and all this stuff that these large language models are already trained on. So we did not have to pay NVIDIA a whole bunch of money to go do custom model training to get this large language model compatibility. Whether you're talking about OpenAI and ChatGPT or Llama or Gemini or any of these big foundation models, they've all been trained out of the box on what Corelight does. And I'd love to tell you that we had some master stroke of strategy to go and enable this; we just got dumb and lucky. But I'll take it, right? Because it's really helping our customers take advantage of the automation that these large language models provide to give them the defenders' leverage and the defenders' advantage. Because we know the attackers are using it, like we talked about before. So the defenders can now use these language models out of the box, independent of all the things we're doing to help them, to go automate their response, which is just incredibly important to match the cadence and speed of the automation the attackers are driving. Is there a risk that this combination of open source plus ChatGPT, Gemini, etc., robs you of potential customers? Or maybe it's a small business, they don't have the money to pay you out right, so they send a junior-level employee to play around on OpenAI and build their own code or build their own security model? Is that a risk factor? Not a risk factor at all for us in this industry. So if you think about the challenge that our customers have, every one of them, whether they have hundreds or thousands of people, getting more out of those people and handling more of the inbound attack volume and being deeper into their risk stack is their number one problem. So I have not talked to a single customer, a single defender who says, "I can adopt AI and I can hire less people." I have not heard those words out of a single human being. Has anyone said, "I can adopt AI and I don't need to work with you"? Oh, no, not at all. Because what they need, think about how that three weeks to three hours conversation we had earlier. If you used to have weeks and now you have hours to respond, the only way to solve that problem is to automate the daylights out of your response. So we have customers today, and by the way, that automation needs the right data. They need that ground truth of what's actually happening to find that attacker. You know, think about what you can get out of ChatGPT if you don't give it any context about you or what you're trying to solve. You'll get generic ideation, but you're not gonna get anything useful you can use. Same thing is true in cyber. Right? If you do generic questions to a large language model, even one that's been tuned for cyber, you don't get much useful. You get general guidance. If you give it unique data about your environment, now it can do something interesting. We are a data provider into these large language models, and we take advantage of that compatibility, and our customers absolutely need to be doing extreme automation to keep up with these cadence of attackers. And to put a number on that, kind of again, this is live from a customer advisory board just a couple months ago, we have customers today who are automating the response to 95% of their inbound threats on a 15-second SLA. That's how fast you have to go, and that's what percentage you have to automate to try and keep up when the attackers are doing the same thing. I want to transition to your journey to CEO, because you did not start at this company as CEO. You joined running product. It can be hard to take over for a founder CEO. Did you have pause? I didn't because, and boy, you are 100% right. There's all sorts of stories of very challenging either founder chemistry or very challenging CEO transitions. And I, I can give 100% of the credit to the successful transition we had to Greg Bell, who was the founding CEO. Because he's one of those rare people that has an incredible combination of IQ and EQ. And he had always said to himself and to the board, "I will take myself out of this chair before you do." And we were at a point, you know, we were passing through about 20 million of revenue, and he said, "Look, we're kind of evolving from fly-by-sight to fly-by-numbers." And he said, "Look, I don't want to fly by numbers. I want to fly by sight. I want to be in the fight. I don't want to be building systems and scaling. I want to be in the action, kind of in with customers, in with the community. That's what gets him excited." And the first thing we tried to do, myself included, was talk him out of that. Because bear in mind, this is the middle of COVID. Company loves him, high amount of personal loyalty, we're in the middle of COVID, we need a CEO transition like I need a hole in my head. Right? Like, no thanks. And my comment to Greg was, "Look, this is not going to be a challenge. I can whisper into your ear everything you need to know to scale this company for the next two years. Stay in the seat." And he said, "Brian, I love you, no." Right? And it turns up he was brilliant on two levels. One is that he had the EQ to help drive a really successful transition. So again, credit to him. And two is the more commodity job is the CEO job. You can hire a recruiting firm and, you know, screen a bunch of candidates and put them in the cage ring and see who wins. Happy I got the board's confidence on that, but you know, it's a process. He was leading our strategy function and our open source community development efforts. That is a much harder job to fill, actually. Because, you know, open source communities, you don't just go recruit someone and drop them in. That's an earned position of respect. And the fact that he had been such a member, a native member of that community for the past 10, I think even 15 years, that is a much harder skill set and credential to replace than is the CEO. And so both of those things I think really worked to make that transition really elegant. But again, I give so much of the credit to Greg here. It's a really interesting story, because I think it can be hard for some founders to let go of the CEO position and to have the insight to know "This is my baby, but I'm better spent doing this strategic project and bringing someone in to run as CEO." And I think that's good advice for anyone. And I've heard you say you were in an interesting situation when you took over as CEO because you somehow found yourself hiring for four direct reports at the same time. Would you look back on that as a mistake? Well, let's break down the four. One was replacing myself. One was we had a new sales leader which, you know, happens, that is a common thing as you scale. And then I had two of my direct reports decide to retire without knowledge of the other one within the same five calendar days. So I kind of couldn't help but be in the situation. The piece that was absolutely a mistake was I tried to parallel path too many of those recruitments. And if I had to do that over again, I would have been a little more serialized and just put a lot more effort between let's say two of those roles rather than trying to do four recruitments in parallel, especially at the executive level where you've got to put a lot of time and effort into really deeply understanding the person, assessing cultural fit, really understanding what you're buying from a hiring standpoint. And especially the culture piece is critical. Doing four of those in parallel was soul-crushing. That was actually really hard. And I think if I had to do that again, of the many mistakes I've made, I would definitely pick two of them and just put a triage plan in place for the other two and say, "Look, I'm going to park these. You know, I'm going to put interim leaders in place or whatever for a six-month period so I can put all of my effort on the first two." So if I had something to do over again, I would definitely handle how I tackled the recruitment differently. What are the other mistakes that you would go back in time and fix, or advise yourself to fix? You want the long list or the short version? The number one or two. The number one, I think the biggest thing I've learned professionally at Corelight over the nearly eight years I've been there, is I have overhauled how I hire people three different times since I was here. Really? Yes. I thought I knew how to hire. You know, I'd led big teams, you know, two, two and a half thousand folks, kind of multiple different functions. I thought I understood how to hire. When you're in a bigger company and you're not growing at hyper-speed, the amount of hiring you do is radically lower and you have a huge talent ecosystem you're pulling off of. So a bunch of folks are internal transfers, you have a wonderful ability to really get a complete 360 on that person because they're part of your internal labor ecosystem. Right? You can triangulate incredibly easily. Let's compare that to when you're trying to be a 30-person company and double it. Right? You want to go from 30 to 60 people. You gotta hire 30 people. You're probably interviewing five people for each of those and let's say that you do at a bare minimum three interviews. So now you got 15 interview spots for 30 people, that's 450 interviews. How many of those 30 employees you think are good interviewers? It's not all 30 of them. It's probably 10. So now your 10 good interviewers are doing 45 interviews apiece as you try to double the company. So you get really good at interviewing and really good at hiring really quick because you're just doing it a ton, right? You're getting a lot of reps at that exercise. And by the way, you're going to be working incredibly closely with these people and every single one of these hires is consequential to what you're driving, especially in those early stages. Frankly, I would argue even north of 500 people now, it's really every person is still critical as we bring them onboard. So the ability to really rigorously assess cultural fit, the ability to be super open-eyed on what's this person's incredible strength and where can we help them grow, kind of where is this still a good career opportunity for them, and how is this person's stage appropriate for where we are? Because what you need at 2 million versus 50 million versus 100 million plus are often different things. Being so much more aware of every stage of that, if I could give my old self at eight years ago the interview skills I have right now, we would be even better as a company for sure. That's really interesting. Has the landscape of hiring and—here AI enters the conversation again—also affected your outlook and style in interviewing and recruiting? Because we see all the time people using AI to generate a resume or an application and on the flip side recruiters are using AI to screen, and it can create a bit of a mess. Are you entangled in that? A mess is a perfect description of that state. Yeah, I've even had people, because aggressive candidates will just write me directly. They'll guess what my email address is and they'll try to write me directly. I think it was just two weeks ago I had three different candidates for the same job write me a letter and they must have been using the same AI tool because the letters were shockingly similar, clearly autogenerated. Yeah, so there's a lot of noise in there. You have to cut through the noise. That's just part of the cat and mouse game of kind of these different tools working together. But I think the two biggest things that have been impactful for us is the hiring markets move with the stock markets, right? So you know gross unemployment will dictate how aggressively people are looking or not. So we think of it as: what's your buy-to-sell ratio in an interview? Right? You know, how hungry is the candidate for the job based on how the rest of the job market is. Over the last eight years, that has ebbed and flowed for sure. So that's absolutely there. But that doesn't change our interviewing tactics, our interviewing approach. Right? We're very focused on great athletes that can scale with the company. We're really focused on a great track record. We're really focused on cultural fit. Those are super durable; I don't think those are going to change. The piece that has changed, especially for me at the executive team, is the skill set you need at 2 million and 100 million are different. And so adapting how we think about screening for results and who will return our call, right? The candidate pool that will call you back at 2 million—not just will call you back, should call you back—at 2 million and 100 million, those are completely different. So those have really changed kind of how we think about recruiting just because the target pool both of skill set and of respondents is different at those sizes. When you joined revenue was at 2 million and today it's north of 100 million. What's been the biggest mindset shift you've experienced or had to adopt as CEO as revenue has grown to this level? Oh, that's an awesome question by the way. I think for me it's been a bounce. Because bear in mind if you go before the 2 million, I was leading orgs of north of a billion, right, you know, and in the thousands of people. So we went from thousands of people to tens of people, now to the mid-hundreds of people, right? And what I would say is the biggest shift for me is when you run really large organizations you have to be ruthlessly effective at direction setting and delegation. And in fact you have to ask yourself, "Can anyone else do this thing?" Because if anyone else can do this thing someone else should do this thing, I shouldn't. Right? I have to be ruthless about delegating and focus on the things that only I can do. Because otherwise you won't scale as a leader. Then you go into a company that's got 30 people and guess what: you're not delegating, you're doing. You're doing everything. I don't care whether it's, you know, like again you mentioned I joined leading product. I was the day before my CEO promotion I was in what's called a scrum meeting, right, where you're in with the engineering team prioritizing individual tickets, right? Tiny sub-features or bug fixes. And literally my promotion to CEO was announced that afternoon. Right? Because you are doing at a very, very granular level, which I love and appreciate by the way because it's given me such a grounding. But then as the company scales I have to get back into delegation. And not just back into it myself, but I have to help the team around me see that I'm doing this consciously. They have to see that I don't want the decisions to roll up to me. They have to see that I want them to assume authority, to drive initiative, to take that charge, and to be super comfortable that they're not going to be perfect because I wasn't going to be perfect either. Right? One of the great pieces of advice from one of our board members was "I can raise more money, I can't raise more time." And there's an illusion that look, if I slow down I'll get it right more often. I don't buy that. Right? There is a pace of urgency you can do where you're just being sloppy, sure. There, slow down a little bit, don't be sloppy. But most of the mistakes we make aren't because we are out of time, it's because we don't know. Right? Buying yourself more time doesn't help you solve a mistake you can't see coming. And so we're way better off being disciplined, being smarter about our decisions, but driving those decisions, encouraging authority, encouraging creativity. Be focused on the customer, be focused on the community, be focused on our fellow Corelighter for sure. But take the decision and run with it. And I need to help my team see that I want them to do that and I don't want the decisions to come back to me. Because it's this bounce back towards delegation. But now I've got a brand equity; some of these folks were working with me when we were 20% of our size, and they've seen me in a very different operating model. So it's like I have to change my own brand image within the company as we scale. I feel like we could talk about leadership advice for hours, but we're coming up on time. So I want to leave you with a final thought. If we speak one year from now, what would you hope to report to the Forbes audience that Corelight has accomplished or that open source technology has helped the world's organizations and companies do in terms of data protection in a world of increasing cybersecurity attacks? You know, my team makes fun of me that I always answer things in threes. So I'll try to live true to my brand reputation to our earlier conversation on that one. I would say three things. First, I would hope that Corelight as a company has continued to do what we're trying to do right now, which is to take this insight and this heritage of serving some of the most elite defenders in the world and continue to bring that insight down to the much broader enterprise. Because there's such a lesson learned from those elite defenders that we can package and really benefit the much broader economy. Right? So that's number one. Number two, from a community standpoint, to continue to serve and deepen the roots that are helping us in turn be successful. Right? So to have the community, because there's a bunch of formerly open source projects that have gone closed source—there's a whole religious set of debates going on about that and have been over the last five years—but for our community to see that we still have their back. That would be number two. And number three, I would love to stand here and say that we have successfully created career-defining opportunities for my fellow Corelighters at the company as we continue to grow. Because folks don't just join companies because of great missions. And we feel like we have a great mission from a cybersecurity perspective, but they join because they have great opportunities, right? That the culture feels good and they want to grow their careers and learn and come out as better human beings and they continue to drive their careers afterwards. If I and if we as a company can deliver those three things, you know, for our customers, for the community, and for our fellow Corelighters, that'll be a fantastic year. Brian Dye, CEO of Corelight, thank you so much for joining us. We really appreciate your time and your insight. Thank you. Love the conversation, Maggie. Appreciate it.

Sam Altman Unfiltered: ChatGPT, AI Risks &amp; What’s Coming Next, 40 Questions in 60 Minutes

The Indian Express(4.3M) ·59:56 ·303.9K views · 2026-02-20
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
Sam Altman reports that AI has evolved from high-school competency to research-level mathematics in just one year, signaling that AGI and Superintelligence (ASI) are approaching faster than previously anticipated.
TL;DR Sam Altman reports that AI has evolved from high-school competency to research-level mathematics in just one year, signaling that AGI and Superintelligence (ASI) are approaching faster than previously anticipated. He identifies India as a surging "builder" market rather...

TL;DR

Sam Altman reports that AI has evolved from high-school competency to research-level mathematics in just one year, signaling that AGI and Superintelligence (ASI) are approaching faster than previously anticipated. He identifies India as a surging "builder" market rather than just a consumer, while emphasizing that global democratization and massive infrastructure investment are the only safeguards against the dangerous concentration of AI power.

AI Evolution and the Path to Superintelligence

  • Rapid Capability Gains: In one year, AI progressed from solving high school math to solving 7 out of 10 previously unsolved research-level mathematical problems and making breakthroughs in physics.
  • Proximity to AGI/ASI: Altman notes that Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) is "pretty close" and the timeline for Artificial Superintelligence (ASI) has accelerated due to a "faster takeoff" than originally predicted.
  • Coding Revolution: The role of the programmer has changed more in the last year than in the previous two decades. Tools like Codex now allow users to build entire applications from a single prompt, shifting human labor to higher levels of abstraction.
  • The "Science Discovery" Narrative: Altman attributes OpenAI’s success not to management, but to a small group of researchers who discovered a deep algorithm that scales indefinitely with more compute.

India’s Strategic Position in AI

  • Shift from Consumer to Builder: Altman observed a massive shift in India since 2023, noting that "builder energy is off the charts" and India is now the fastest-growing market for Codex globally.
  • Economic Vulnerabilities: While acknowledging that AI poses a threat to India’s IT service sector (8% of GDP), Altman argues that "leisure is a myth" and new, unpredictable jobs will emerge, provided the workforce remains adaptable and resilient.
  • Foundation Model Controversy: Clarifying past remarks, Altman stated that while $10 million is insufficient to build a frontier model today, India is uniquely positioned to lead in "narrow models" and vertical integration across the AI stack.

Infrastructure and Global Geopolitics

  • The Compute Crisis: Altman estimates the world may eventually need trillions of GPUs to serve 8 billion people. This will require the "most expensive, complex infrastructure project" in human history.
  • Energy Requirements: He dismissed concerns about water usage as "fake" but admitted energy consumption is a major hurdle, necessitating a rapid global shift toward nuclear, wind, and solar power.
  • China vs. Democracy:
      • The Real Threat: Rather than "humanoid robot armies," Altman fears new types of cyber-warfare and the use of AI to create a surveillance state.
      • China’s Edge: China leads in physical robotics, electric motors, and energy build-out, while the U.S. currently leads in model sophistication.
  • Democratization vs. Concentration: Altman warned against any single country, company, or individual (including himself or the U.S.) having exclusive control over superintelligence.

Governance, Safety, and Corporate Strategy

  • Regulatory Focus: Governments should regulate "catastrophic risks" rather than stifling innovation at the application level. He warns against using safety as an excuse for the concentration of power.
  • Hardware Teaser: OpenAI is collaborating with Jony Ive to develop a new hardware form factor designed specifically for natural language interaction, moving away from the "pointer and window" UI used for the last 50 years.
  • OpenAI’s Internal Shift: Altman admitted that not taking equity in OpenAI was a "dumb decision" that fueled conspiracy theories. He reaffirmed that OpenAI is a "research-first" company that views the product as a byproduct of scientific discovery.
  • Corporate Nimbleness: He criticized large corporations planning multi-year AI strategies, stating that a timeline for deployment in 2028 is a "catastrophic mistake" given the current pace of change.
"I don't think there should be any one person or any one country or any one company in charge of superintelligence, including the United States. I think the world is at its best when power is widely distributed." — Sam Altman
"Writing C++ code by hand? That's over. Big categories of jobs AI is just going to completely obsolete, and what those people do will have to completely adapt." — Sam Altman
"We have used computers the same way for 50 years... Hardware is hard, but we have a chance to make a new family of products that are participating in your life and not in the way of it." — Sam Altman
Sam, I'll just wait for the audience to settle down a bit. Guys, it's a very special day for us. Forbes magazine calls OpenAI one of the most important companies in the world. And the man who is... if we look back at today 20 years from now, we'll probably... I think I'll just wait for the audience to settle down a bit. Okay? Okay, are we settled? Yeah. If we look back at today 20 years from now, I don't think it'll be an overstatement to say that the gentleman in front of me today is probably that one person who has made a tectonic shift. If anything close to the Industrial Revolution, it probably is the gentleman in front of me right now who it all probably started with. Is that a fair statement to make, Sam? No. You know, I think this is generally true for many companies. The person running the company gets way too much credit relative to the work everybody else does. But in our case in particular, this has really been a story of a scientific discovery. There have been a handful of researchers that did a miracle and figured out a very deep thing about the way the world works. And the thing that is so special about deep learning is what has led it to be so general, which is that we figured out—we, this small set of researchers—figured out this algorithm that could learn anything. And at scale, it gets better and better. And then a whole set of people have worked very hard to deliver that scale on these models. And then the whole world has figured out how to build incredible products and services around that. But more than anything, this is a story of the scientists. Yeah. And when you've... the last time you were in India to now, a lot has changed in the world, but what do you think has changed in India with respect to AI? It was just a little over a year ago that I was here. And before talking about what's changed in India, I think it's worth talking about what's changed in AI, because this will ground this. A year ago, AI was just able to do high school math. And this was incredible. AI could do a, you know, a very good, not an excellent but a very good job at high school mathematics. And people couldn't believe it. And I was in India, I was speaking at an event like this and I was talking about it. And people were... it had just happened. People genuinely felt wonder about it. They're like, "You know, this can do what an 11th grader can do. This is amazing." Because only a couple years before that, you know, AI couldn't really, like, do any math at all. It couldn't do grade school math; it was struggling with that. By last summer, it was competing at the hardest mathematics competitions we have in the world and doing okay. And last week, there was a new thing called First Proof, where mathematicians put out 10 research problems that the solutions to which were not known, and it took our best mathematicians, the cutting edge of research, you know, new knowledge to figure out because it hadn't yet been shared. And I believe our latest AI got seven of those problems right. So AI has gone from doing okay at high school math to being able to do new research-level mathematics, figure out new knowledge. This has also happened in physics. And this is an amazing change in a year. We've gone from AI that could do what we expected a very smart high school student to do to pushing the edge of human knowledge. The other... so that's change number one. The other change is that AI has gone from being able to write a little bit of programming to completely changing what it means to be a computer programmer. Last time I was here, people were amazed at these sort of auto-complete tools for code. And now if you type in an idea, one year later, if you type in an idea in something like Codex, you can have an entire application created. The job of a programmer is now, I think, in this last year changed more than it's changed in any year that I've been an adult. Codex is our... India is our fastest-growing market for Codex. So especially what's happening here, to get to the next part of the question, is really amazing. The biggest change that I feel from last time I was here is when I was here last time it felt like India was a consumer of AI. People were using these services, people were doing other stuff. And now the builder energy in India is off the charts. And you in fact said something a little controversial last time. Now it's controversial because at that time you said, you know, you had painted a picture of despair that, you know, a $10 million fund would not be enough to, you know, to build an LLM, a meaningful LLM out of India. And now yesterday we're talking about a full stack coming out of India and India can actually lead in AI. Yeah. That comment, I mean that got taken out of context. That was a few years ago. But the comment was not that India couldn't do an amazing job with a $10 million company and a $10 million model. I think that clearly is the case. It was to say... could you make a very frontier model for $10 million? I think you couldn't then, I think it's even more true now that you can't. They've gotten very expensive. LIVE: Cisco CEO Addresses India AI Impact Summit 2026

India Today Global(151.0K) ·155:29 ·199 views · 2026-02-20
Jeetu Patel (CEO, Cisco)
Cisco CEO Jeetu Patel asserts that the global AI transition requires a fundamental shift from document-based governance to runtime security, positioning India as a central hub for this evolution due to its massive scale, digital foundation, and young talent pool.
TL;DR Cisco CEO Jeetu Patel asserts that the global AI transition requires a fundamental shift from document-based governance to runtime security, positioning India as a central hub for this evolution due to its massive scale, digital foundation, and young talent pool. Machine...

TL;DR

Cisco CEO Jeetu Patel asserts that the global AI transition requires a fundamental shift from document-based governance to runtime security, positioning India as a central hub for this evolution due to its massive scale, digital foundation, and young talent pool.

The Shift from Intelligence to Agency

  • Machine Data Dominance: Approximately 55% of global data growth is now machine-generated (logs, metrics, events, and traces).
      • AI agents require this specific data type to operate with sufficient context in a 24/7 environment.
  • Workflow Transformation: AI should not be used to merely augment broken processes.
      • Process Re-engineering: Organizations must fundamentally rethink and adjust their workflows to accommodate agents, rather than expecting agents to adjust to human legacy processes.
  • Contextual Enrichment: Success in the "AI era" depends on agents being enriched with deep context to ensure they operate effectively within specific business environments.

Resolving the "Trust Deficit" in Autonomous AI

  • Action vs. Answer Risk: The primary risk has shifted; the danger is no longer just AI providing a "wrong answer," but AI taking a "wrong action."
  • Bi-directional Protection Strategy:
      • Protecting Agents from the World: Defending against jailbreaking, prompt injection attacks, tool abuse, and data poisoning.
      • Protecting the World from Agents: Implementing dynamic guardrails to prevent agents from going "rogue" or exhibiting unintended behaviors.
  • Runtime Governance: Patel argues that governance can no longer be a static document; it must be a "runtime implementation" where guardrails are injected into the system as the agent operates.

Cisco’s Infrastructure and Full-Stack Observability

  • Critical Infrastructure: Cisco is developing networks specifically designed for agents to run on, focusing on simplicity, security, and context-enrichment.
  • End-to-End Visibility: The strategy relies on "bottom to top" observability across the entire stack.
      • Monitoring Layers: This includes GPU utilization, model performance, application architecture, and agent behavior.
  • Secure Token Generation: The goal is to ensure every company and country can generate AI tokens in the most effective and secure manner possible.

India’s Role as a Global AI Shaper

  • Strategic Advantages: India is positioned to lead AI development globally, rather than just being a consumer of the technology.
      • Demographic Dividend: India possesses one of the world's largest workforces under the age of 30.
      • Digital Foundation: Rare large-scale common identities and payment systems like Aadhaar and UPI provide a unique data foundation.
      • Data Scale: Because AI performance scales with data volume, India’s massive population provides a distinct competitive edge.
  • Human-AI Collaboration: Patel emphasizes that the future is built when humans can confidently delegate tasks to AI within a safe ecosystem.
"The risk with AI is AI is going to take the wrong action. And when you actually start having an AI take the wrong action, the consequences are far more grave than just giving you the wrong answer." — Jeetu Patel
"Agents don't adjust to us, we have to adjust our process to the agents so that they can actually be effective for us." — Jeetu Patel
"No longer is governance a document. It’s going to be a runtime implementation... we have to be able to inject guard rails into that dynamically at runtime." — Jeetu Patel

You have to fundamentally rethink the process that accommodates these agents. Agents don't adjust to us; we have to adjust our process to the agents so that they can actually be effective for us. So, that's the second big area: the context gap. And then the third area that we talked about was this notion of a trust deficit. In AI, the risk now is no longer that AI is going to give us the wrong answer. The risk with AI is that AI is going to take the wrong action. And when you actually start having an AI take the wrong action, the consequences are far more grave than just giving you the wrong answer. So, what do we have to do? There are two areas that we think are going to be really important. The first one is we've got to make sure that these agents get protected from the world. Which means that jailbreaking an agent, having prompt injection attacks, making sure that there's a level of tool abuse or data poisoning—we have to make sure that we can protect the agents from that happening. And then the second thing we have to do is we have to make sure that we can protect the world from the agents. So that if the agent starts going rogue, or it starts having behavior that's unintended, we can actually make sure that we can provide effective guardrails at runtime. Because no longer is governance a document; it's going to be a runtime implementation. So that as the agent is working, if you start seeing that the agent's doing something that's not going to be in the best interest of humans, we have to be able to inject guardrails into that dynamically at runtime so that we can make sure that that creates a level of trust for the system. Now, it turns out that Cisco is actually building solutions across all of these three areas. And so, the way that we think about ourselves is we want to invent and innovate in making sure that the critical infrastructure for the AI era is as simple to deploy, as safe and secure as we want it to be, and as context enriched as it can be. So, what are we doing? We're building networks that agents will run on. We're building context that makes it richer for these agents to operate in a way that allows us to make sure that we can delegate safely and securely to them and feel good about the outcomes that we're going to get. And we're going to make sure that we're going to have security that governs these agents. And all of this is going to be done with a tremendous amount of observability and visibility that says at every layer of the stack—from the way that the GPU is getting utilized, to how the model is performing, to how the apps are being built, to how the agents are performing—we can have observability from bottom to top, the entire stack. Because if we can do that and figure out that every company, every country is generating tokens in the most effective and secure way, then you're going to see that there's progress being made. Now why is this a tremendous opportunity for India? Because India is not going to just use AI. The way that we've seen over the course of the past week is you actually helping shape the direction of the entire world with AI. And I think there's a few key areas that we should all feel very hopeful for and why India can be a tremendous contributor to AI for the rest of the world. Not just for India, but for the entire world. The first one is we have a huge talent pool of young, vibrant, intelligent, smart, educated people within India that actually contribute to the workforce. We've got one of the largest groups of people under 30 that are actually going out and contributing to the economy. Number two is we have a very strong digital foundation. Having common identity with Aadhaar, having the UPI—these are things that, you know, we in India, you all might take for granted, but these are very rare to come by in countries, especially at scale. And third, India actually has massive, massive scale. And why is that important? Because AI works best with scale. Because AI works best when you have the most amount of data. Right? And so the way I think about this is we have a tremendous opportunity ahead of us. Now, the future is not going to be built by AI alone. In fact, the future gets built when humans can confidently put AI to work and delegate jobs and tasks to AI in a way that we feel safe and secure. And so I'm actually as hopeful as I've ever been. However, I also feel like there's a tremendous amount of risks of these things going sideways. And so we as a community have to band together and make sure that we actually work as an ecosystem to keep AI safe and secure. Because if we do that in the right way, we're going to solve the hardest problems that humanity has faced and reduce and hopefully end suffering in so many different areas. We might be able to cure the best, the hardest diseases that we've not been able to overcome. We might be able to overcome poverty.
