IMF Chief Kristalina Georgieva Exclusive Interview on India&#39;s AI Moment | World Exclusive Interview

India Today(11.0M) ·21:19 ·100 views · 2026-02-20
Kristalina Georgieva (Managing Director, IMF) Siddharth Zarabi (Journalist, India Today)
AI represents a "tsunami" for the global labor market that could boost global growth by 0.8%, but its benefits must be democratized through digital public infrastructure and robust social safety nets to prevent a widening gap between "haves" and "have-nots.
TL;DR AI represents a "tsunami" for the global labor market that could boost global growth by 0.8%, but its benefits must be democratized through digital public infrastructure and robust social safety nets to prevent a widening gap between "haves" and "have-nots." The Labor...

TL;DR

AI represents a "tsunami" for the global labor market that could boost global growth by 0.8%, but its benefits must be democratized through digital public infrastructure and robust social safety nets to prevent a widening gap between "haves" and "have-nots."

AI’s Impact on Global Labor and Productivity

  • The Labor "Tsunami": AI is expected to impact 40% of jobs globally and 60% in advanced economies, either through enhancement or replacement.
      • The "Middle Squeeze": While AI creates high-paying tech jobs and indirectly stimulates low-skilled service demand (restaurants, hotels), it threatens to eliminate middle-tier and entry-level roles.
      • Wage Disparity: Early data from the U.S. shows 1 in 10 jobs now requires AI-enhanced skills and offers higher pay, potentially widening income inequality.
  • Economic Growth Potential: AI has the potential to break the current cycle of "low growth, high debt" by adding an estimated 0.8% to global GDP, potentially returning growth to pre-pandemic levels.
  • Policy Prescriptions:
      • Education Reform: Moving toward "learning to learn" and fostering adaptability rather than static skill sets.
      • Flexicurity: Georgieva recommends the Danish "flexicurity" model, which combines labor market flexibility for employers with strong social security for displaced workers.

The Indian Model of AI Democratization

  • Digital Public Infrastructure (DPI): Georgieva highlights India’s success in creating a Digital ID for 1.4 billion people as the foundation for accessible AI.
  • Public Good vs. Corporate Models: India’s approach of building foundational models for public sectors like healthcare and education is cited as a more applicable blueprint for the Global South than the high-tech corporate models of the U.S. or China.
  • Global Leadership: India is positioned to lead the developing world in ensuring AI remains a source of benefit for all society rather than a tool for further fragmentation.

Navigating Regulation and the Global Trade Order

  • Regulation vs. Innovation: The MD warns against "suffocating" innovation with excessive red tape, though she emphasizes the need for collective ethical guardrails to ensure "AI for good, not for evil."
  • The Multipolar Trade Reality: Despite rising tariffs and fragmentation, Georgieva argues that "trade is like water"—it will find a way around obstacles.
      • Regional Integration: Global trade is shifting toward bilateral and plurilateral agreements (e.g., India’s deals with the UK, EU, and US).
      • Supply Chain Continuity: Total decoupling is viewed as impossible; supply chains will continue to cross borders despite geopolitical tensions.

Global Economic Risks and Fiscal Fragility

  • Exhausted Buffers: A primary concern is that many countries have exhausted their fiscal capacity due to the pandemic, war in Ukraine, and high interest rates.
      • The "Shock-Prone" World: Unlike previous crises, rich countries currently lack the "tons of money" needed to provide positive spillovers to the rest of the world when the next shock hits.
  • Climate Change: Georgieva describes climate change as a critical risk but also a leadership opportunity for India, specifically through initiatives like the International Solar Alliance.
  • IMF’s Role: The IMF currently manages 50 programs (including Sri Lanka) to support countries facing high debt and diminished official development assistance.
"AI is like a tsunami hitting the labor market. We assess 40% of jobs globally, 60% in advanced economies, to be impacted by AI in the next years, either enhanced, made more productive, or replaced." — Kristalina Georgieva
"What India is doing in AI is phenomenal because the goal of India is to democratize AI... India is determined to help the rest of the world, especially the Global South, to benefit from AI." — Kristalina Georgieva
"Trade is like water, you put an obstacle, it goes around it. My projection is the following: we are in a multipolar world. This is a reality." — Kristalina Georgieva
At the AI Impact Summit in New Delhi, International Monetary Fund Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva highlighted India’s unique approach to the rapid advancement of artificial intelligence. A central theme of the summit was the democratization of AI, reflecting India’s determination to ensure the technology serves as a source of benefit for the entire society rather than a force that widens the gap between the haves and the have-nots. Georgieva noted that the speed of AI’s integration into work and life is the highest in the history of technological transformation, presenting a tremendous opportunity for productivity and growth while simultaneously posing risks of worker displacement. The impact of AI on the labor market is likened to a tsunami, with IMF assessments suggesting that 40% of jobs globally—and 60% in advanced economies—will be affected. This transformation creates a "middle squeeze," where high-skilled jobs are enhanced and pay more, and demand for low-skilled service jobs increases, but mid-level roles are often eliminated. To counter these risks, policymakers are encouraged to redefine education for an era of continuous learning, strengthen social safety nets, and adopt "flexicurity" models that combine labor market flexibility with security for workers. India’s recent labor market reforms were noted as a positive step in creating this necessary adaptability. India’s strategy in the global AI landscape is built upon its robust digital public infrastructure. By creating digital IDs for 1.4 billion people, India has established a foundation to make AI more accessible and applicable to public goods such as healthcare and education. This model may prove more applicable to the Global South than the high-tech corporate models seen in the United States or China. While the IMF projects that AI could potentially lift global growth by 0.8%, reaching this potential requires international cooperation on ethical guardrails to ensure the technology is used for good while avoiding suffocating innovation with excessive regulation. Beyond AI, the global economic landscape is shifting toward a multipolar reality. While new tariff orders and trade fragmentations create complexity, global trade continues to adapt through regional and plurilateral agreements. Trade remains resilient because the world is too integrated to fully pull apart. However, the world has become more shock-prone, dealing with the lingering effects of the pandemic, international conflicts, and climate change. Many countries have exhausted their fiscal buffers, making global solidarity and the role of the IMF crucial for supporting nations in distress. India remains a global bright spot with domestic growth around 7%, contributing significantly to the world economy. Although climate change remains a universal concern, it offers India an opportunity for leadership through initiatives like the International Solar Alliance. By maintaining a dynamic, innovation-driven economy, India serves as a model for other nations navigating the transition into a high-tech, shock-prone global environment.

Prince Andrew Arrested, Epstein Mythology, Reid Hoffman Files with Saagar Enjeti & Michael Tracey

TL;DR

The Jeffrey Epstein case has evolved into a global scandal characterized by either an indictment of ruling-class impunity or a media-driven "mythology" fueled by financial incentives and moral panic. While recent developments like the arrest of Prince Andrew highlight institutional corruption, skeptics argue the narrative of a "global pedophile ring" lacks the evidentiary rigor required for such a massive claim.

The Arrest of Prince Andrew and Institutional Accountability

  • Prince Andrew's Arrest: Saagar Enjeti views the recent arrest of Prince Andrew in the UK—nominally for mishandling trade secrets—as a targeted move following revelations that he shared non-public trade information with Epstein.
  • The "Epstein Class": Enjeti argues that Epstein belonged to a specific class of individuals who operate above the law, utilizing high-level connections to secure "sweetheart deals," such as the 2007 non-prosecution agreement.
  • The Intelligence Nexus:
      • Surveillance and Spying: Enjeti points to evidence of the Israeli government installing surveillance in Epstein-owned apartments and Epstein’s own attempts to FOIA the CIA regarding his 1980s arms-dealing associations.
      • Financial Mastery: Epstein is described as a "master money launderer" whose value to intelligence agencies lay in his ability to move money surreptitiously across the globe.

The "Epstein Mythology" and Media Hysteria

  • Moral Panic: Michael Tracey argues that the current Epstein narrative resembles the "Satanic Panic" of the 1980s. He contends that the public has been "bamboozled" by a media-driven frenzy that prioritizes algorithmic engagement over evidentiary standards.
  • Credibility of Key Accusers:
      • Virginia Roberts Giuffre (VRG): Tracey highlights that VRG’s own lawyers admitted her memoir manuscript was a "fictionalized account." He notes she recanted sexual misconduct allegations against Alan Dershowitz and others after intense litigation.
      • Unreliable Testimony: Tracey identifies other prominent accusers, such as Maria Farmer and Sarah Ransome, as "mentally unwell" individuals whose claims of a "rape island" have never been backed by credible, contemporaneous evidence of rape.
  • The Settlement Industry:
      • Financial Incentives: Tracey estimates the "Epstein industry" is worth roughly $1 billion. He suggests that massive payouts from the Epstein estate, JPMorgan ($290M), and Deutsche Bank ($80M) incentivize individuals to "reimagine" past encounters as sex trafficking for tax-free compensation.

Financial Networks and the Wexner Connection

  • The Les Wexner Mystery: David Sacks and Saagar Enjeti discuss the "bizarre" relationship between Epstein and L Brands founder Les Wexner.
      • Financial Control: Wexner granted Epstein full power of attorney in 1991, allowing him near-total control over his multi-billion dollar fortune—a move Sacks notes is highly irregular for high-net-worth individuals.
      • Early Wealth: Enjeti posits that Epstein’s lifestyle "exploded" in 1992 following this connection, though Wexner now claims he was "conned" by Epstein.
  • Technological Foresight: Epstein showed an early interest in Bitcoin as early as 2011, reaching out to Jason Calacanis to meet Bitcoin Core founders. Enjeti views this as further evidence of Epstein's obsession with surreptitious financial technologies.

Political Weaponization and Media Bias

  • Reid Hoffman’s Deflection: David Sacks criticizes tech billionaire Reid Hoffman for "throwing stones while living in a glass house."
      • Partisan Shielding: Sacks argues that Hoffman has aggressively accused others (like Elon Musk and Donald Trump) of Epstein ties to retreat into the protection of the Democratic party "tribe."
      • Inconsistent Reporting: Sacks notes the *New York Times* and other mainstream outlets have given Hoffman a "pass," minimizing his extensive history with Epstein while highlighting others with far fewer connections.
  • Guilt by Association: Both Sacks and Tracey agree that any association with Epstein has become "radioactive," leading public figures to lie about their relationships even if no crime was committed.

Notable Quotes

"Jeffrey Epstein was a master money launderer and financial mastermind... who was at the forefront of the Bitcoin technology in 2011... This shows how he was at the forefront of new technology and new ways to move money surreptitiously across the globe." — Saagar Enjeti
"This whole notion [of a global pedo-crime ring] is based on just an onslaught of mythological nonsense that's pumped out daily... driven by these perverse algorithmic incentives to be totally divorced from the facts." — Michael Tracey
"Reid Hoffman came out on X and very aggressively started pointing the finger at other people... This is just a classic case of someone throwing stones while living in a glass house. He lies about the extent of his relationship with Epstein... while again wildly accusing other people." — David Sacks
"We're in this other domain of like who can establish the most damning guilt by association even though nobody can spell out what the guilt is supposed to be tied to." — Michael Tracey

LIVE: Treasury Secretary Bessent speaks at Economic Club of Dallas

Reuters(4.0M) ·72:26 ·6.1K views · 2026-02-20
Scott Bessent (Treasury Secretary)
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlined a national strategy of "Economic Security" focused on industrial reshoring, technological dominance in AI, and financial inclusion through "Trump Accounts," while confirming the administration will bypass rec...
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlined a national strategy of "Economic Security" focused on industrial reshoring, technological dominance in AI, and financial inclusion through "Trump Accounts," while confirming the administration will bypass recent Supreme Court...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent outlined a national strategy of "Economic Security" focused on industrial reshoring, technological dominance in AI, and financial inclusion through "Trump Accounts," while confirming the administration will bypass recent Supreme Court restrictions on IEEPA tariffs by utilizing alternative legal authorities (Sections 232 and 301).

The Three Pillars of Economic Security

Secretary Bessent defined economic security as the foundation of national sovereignty, structured around three primary objectives:

  • Industrial and Technological Dominance: The administration is prioritizing the reshoring of strategic industries to eliminate "single points of failure."
      • Manufacturing Resilience: Bessent highlighted the loss of 6 million manufacturing jobs between 1999 and 2011 as a critical vulnerability exposed by the COVID-19 pandemic.
      • Technological Edge: AI and quantum computing are viewed as "existential" battlegrounds. Every $300 billion in AI investment represents roughly 1% of U.S. GDP.
  • Investment in America: Maintaining the U.S. dollar as the world’s reserve currency and the Treasury market as the global "risk-free" benchmark.
      • Regulatory Reform: Treasury aims to pivot from "constraint-focused" regulation to frameworks that mobilize capital toward semiconductors, energy infrastructure, and defense.
  • Preparedness and Deterrence:
      • Critical Vulnerabilities: Bessent identified two major frontiers of risk: massive cyber incidents affecting financial systems and the fact that 90% of advanced chips are still manufactured in Taiwan.
      • Economic Statecraft: "America First" does not mean "America Alone"; the U.S. will work with G20 partners and allies like the UK, Japan, and the EU to promote reciprocal growth.

Tariff Strategy and the Supreme Court Ruling

Addressing the Supreme Court's 6-3 ruling against the use of the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) for revenue generation, Bessent clarified the administration’s response:

  • Legal Pivot: The administration will replace IEEPA-based tariffs by invoking Section 232 (national security), Section 301 (unfair trade practices), and Section 122 (balance of payments) authorities.
  • Revenue Impact: Treasury estimates that switching to these validated legal authorities will result in "virtually unchanged" tariff revenue for 2026.
  • Global Tariffs: Despite the ruling, the President intends to proceed with a 10% global tariff, using the five-month window provided by Section 122 while other investigations are completed.

Expanding Ownership: Trump Accounts

A centerpiece of the briefing was the "Trump Accounts" initiative, designed to ensure the 40% of Americans currently without equity exposure can participate in wealth creation.

  • Seed Funding: Every eligible American child will receive a $1,000 Treasury-funded seed investment at birth, placed in a diversified index tied to U.S. economic growth.
  • Philanthropic Support: Texas natives Michael and Susan Dell have committed $6.25 billion to "top up" accounts for 25 million children in the bottom 80% of income zip codes.
  • Long-term Growth: Funds are locked for 18 years but can be rolled into retirement accounts or used for major life milestones like business startups or home down payments.

Financial System and Banking Reform

Bessent addressed the "regulatory morass" affecting both community banks and the burgeoning private credit market:

  • Community Banking Support: To prevent the flight of deposits to "too big to fail" institutions, the administration supports raising deposit insurance to $10 million for certain non-interest-bearing accounts (e.g., business payroll accounts).
  • Private Credit Oversight: While acknowledging that private credit bridged a gap during tight regulatory periods, Bessent expressed concern about potential contagion. Treasury is monitoring the relationship between regulated banks and private credit funds.
  • Retail Protections: Bessent warned that individual 401(k) investors must not become a "dumping ground" for bad private equity assets or "continuation funds" that lack transparency.
  • Digital Finance: The administration supports a well-regulated, dollar-based stablecoin market to extend the dollar’s influence into emerging digital payment systems.
"Economic security above all else is the foundation of sovereignty and thus the grantor of prosperity." — Scott Bessent
"A nation that cannot produce the critical goods and resources to sustain itself is exposed and vulnerable to coercion." — Scott Bessent
"Despite the misplaced gloating from Democrats... the court did not rule against President Trump's tariffs. Six justices simply ruled that IEEPA authorities cannot be used to raise even one dollar of revenue. This administration will invoke alternative legal authorities." — Scott Bessent
"People who own part of the system don't want to bring down the system. [Trump Accounts] will be the most important program since Social Security in terms of financial security." — Scott Bessent
Club of Dallas, whose leadership and commitment made it possible to bring the Secretary to Dallas for this important conversation. On behalf of everyone here, I would like to thank the Secretary for his leadership, his service, and for spending time with us. It's a privilege to welcome him today. Good afternoon and thank you. I'd like to thank Ray Washburne, Michael Levy, and the entire leadership of the Economic Club of Dallas for hosting me today. As the business leaders of this community, you all know better than anyone that Dallas is a city built by risk-takers, created by a hydrocarbon base, now stacked with Fortune 500 headquarters, and sustained by the simple conviction that prosperity follows production. Texas reflects what a diversified economy looks like, with strength in sectors ranging from advanced manufacturing to technology to finance. We need look no further than Y'all Street to see the parallel prosperity is alive and well right here in Dallas, one of the fastest growing financial hubs in the country. In many respects, Texas embodies what this administration is working to build nationally: economic security rooted in production, resilience, and long-term durable growth. In that sense, Texas can serve as a model for the rest of the United States. Economic security is the foundation that allows a country to fulfill its most basic obligation of safeguarding its people, safeguarding its people. For a nation to be economically secure, it must possess the productive capacity to withstand shocks, deter adversaries, and deliver rising prosperity to its citizens. Unfortunately, past administrations lost sight of this fundamental truth, allowing faux efficiency gains and short-sighted profit obsession to eclipse security resilience and long-term value. President Trump saw the points of failure decades ago, and he has made clear that we cannot compromise on economic sovereignty, accept structural vulnerabilities, or allow strategic industries to erode as a result of outdated or misguided assumptions. At President Trump's direction, our administration has therefore made economic security the centerpiece of our economic policy. Today, I will share our strategy for putting America first by reprioritizing economic security, which rests on three pillars. First, industrial and technological dominance. Second, investment in America. Third, preparedness. From the earliest days of the Republic, our founding fathers understood that independence required economic security. The ability to produce essential goods, sustain public credit, and foster domestic industry was seen as the foundation of sovereignty by Alexander Hamilton. And it still is. After the World Wars, the United States made deliberate concessions in trade and industrial policy to help allies rebuild and defend against the threat of communist expansion. Those decisions contributed to decades of global stability and growth, but with a trade-off. As markets became freer, they did not become fairer. With the China shock of the 21st century, strategic industries were hollowed out, supply chains consolidated overseas, and we became wholly dependent on single foreign suppliers within certain sectors. In just over a decade, between 1999 and 2011, the United States lost nearly 6 million manufacturing jobs. That led to not only employment loss and wage depression, but also to diminished productive capacity and, importantly, resilience. A nation that cannot produce the critical goods and resources to sustain itself is exposed and vulnerable to coercion. The COVID-19 pandemic fully exposed these vulnerabilities on the world stage for our own people and our adversaries to witness. This administration is encouraging American companies to reduce single points of failure by diversifying their production and, importantly, bringing critical outputs back to the United States. Our policies have compelled firms to reassess their sourcing strategies and pour trillions in new investment back into American manufacturing and strategic sectors. Historic trade agreements have begun resetting the global trade paradigm, and over time, diversified supply chains also reduce inflationary volatility by lowering the risk of sudden disruption. One year into the President's second term, we are already seeing and will continue to see results. Industrial might and technological dominance are mutually dependent and reinforcing, which is why the U.S. must maintain its technological edge. The global economy is undergoing a period of rapid transformation with breakthrough advances in AI, quantum computing, and advanced manufacturing. The countries that adopt and deploy these technologies most effectively will shape the next era of growth, and the world is counting on America to lead, as we always have. For 250 years, American innovation has been our decisive advantage. We are in an existential battle to maintain and accelerate technological dominance. The production and development of AI infrastructure will be crucial to both economic growth and national security in this next industrial revolution. Beyond production, leadership in AI adoption is another crucial component of economic security. At Treasury, through the Financial Stability Oversight Council, we are working with regulators and industry leaders to further responsible AI usage in the financial system. We are optimizing regulation for growth, moving away from a posture solely focused on constraint toward one that recognizes that failure to adopt productivity-enhancing technology is itself a risk. Stablecoins represent another area where leadership matters. A well-regulated dollar-based stablecoin market can reinforce the global role of the U.S. dollar and extend its network effects into emerging digital payment systems. The GENIUS Act provides Treasury with oversight tools to ensure transparency and confidence in this sector. Industrial capacity, technological leadership, and a strong dollar policy taken together form the backbone of U.S. economic sovereignty, which is why they are all fundamental to our strategy. To deliver industrial, technological, and currency strength requires confidence and capital, which brings us to pillar two: investment in America. The United States holds a unique position in the global economy with the dollar as reserve currency, a key factor in enabling economic security. Reserve currency status anchors our borrowing costs, deepens our capital markets, strengthens sanctions actions, and reinforces American leadership in global finance. This rests on confidence in our institutions and, critically, in the health of the U.S. Treasury market. The Treasury market is the foundation of the global financial system as the benchmark risk-free asset, serving as collateral across markets and the channel through which global capital flows into the United States. Preserving the strength, liquidity, and credibility of that market is central to economic security and is not something that can be taken for granted. Investment in America also means ensuring that capital flows to sectors that enhance long-term productivity and strategic resilience. To do so, we are focused on tailoring fit-for-purpose regulation. Over the next six months, Treasury will engage industry, academia, and national security experts to evaluate how supervisory frameworks can better mobilize capital toward sectors critical to national strength: advanced manufacturing, energy infrastructure, semiconductors, and defense innovation. Economic security also depends on broader participation in wealth creation. Almost 40% of Americans today have no exposure to the U.S. equity market. This means they participate in the world's greatest economy as workers and producers, but not as owners. Trump Accounts represent a fundamental rewriting of that arrangement. Under this initiative, every eligible American child will receive a $1,000 Treasury-funded seed investment at birth, invested in a diversified index tied to the long-term growth of the U.S. economy. Additional contributions can come from philanthropists, families, employers, and state governments. The objective is simple: give every child a stake in the American dream from day one. Texas has already demonstrated leadership in advancing this vision. In fact, Texas Senator Ted Cruz authored the precursor to the Trump Accounts provision in the "One Big Beautiful Bill," and Texas natives Michael and Susan Dell led the way in philanthropic giving with their historic 6.25 billion, with a "B," donation to top up Trump Accounts for 25 million American children. The President has called on business leaders and philanthropists all around the country to get involved in this initiative, and today I'm calling on the men and women in this room to do the same. Everything is bigger in Texas, and that should also be true for Trump Accounts, because economic security is strongest when it is broadly shared. The final pillar is preparedness. Economic security requires not only strength but resilience in the face of disruption, which means proactive measures to limit disruption from occurring in the first place. The 1973 Arab oil embargo is a cautionary tale and historic lesson. A geopolitical decision thousands of miles away triggered drastic energy shortages, inflation, economic chaos, and market turmoil here at home. American consumers felt that shock acutely when the price of oil jumped by nearly 300% before the embargo lifted. Equally significant as the pocketbook effect was the geopolitical reckoning: the fact that a foreign power could so fundamentally disrupt the U.S. economy with the stroke of a pen. This is the very definition of a single point of failure. Today, in my opinion, there are two significant risk frontiers. The first is a major cyber incident disrupting banks, payment systems, or other financial market infrastructures. The second is that even despite re-shoring efforts, more than 90% of advanced chips are still manufactured in Taiwan. Creating physical and digital safeguards therefore underpins economic security by preventing geopolitical or operational shocks from venturing into economic disequilibrium and a broader loss of confidence. In today's environment, preparedness must also include deterrence. Preparedness means ensuring that no adversary believes it can hold the American economy hostage or disrupt the well-being of American citizens. Geopolitical risk must be a key factor considered in policymaking across agencies and not treated as an afterthought. Economic statecraft remains central to our strategy, as our administration has reinforced repeatedly. America First does not mean America alone. Our economic security is strengthened when the Western Hemisphere is aligned around free market principles and the rule of law. We are deepening cooperation with partners committed to these principles while working constructively with long-standing allies such as the UK, Japan, and the EU as they modernize regulatory frameworks and pursue growth. As the U.S. hosts the G20 this year, we reject the premise of weakened global growth and intend to advance a results-oriented growth and economic security agenda promoting deregulation, competitiveness, and reciprocal opportunity. On the eve of our great nation's 250th anniversary, we recognize that economic security is foundational to our ability to thrive for the next 250 years. Under President Trump's leadership, we are restoring industrial capacity, reinforcing technological leadership, expanding economic opportunity, and strengthening resilience. We are fundamentally resetting the framework in which the United States participates in the global economy, recognizing that economic security above all else is the foundation of sovereignty and thus the grantor of prosperity. Treasury's mission is clear: to act swiftly and decisively to put America and America's security first. I would also like to take a moment to address today's Supreme Court ruling, and I would note that I did not change a single word in my speech post the ruling. President Trump will always put our national security and Americans first. And as I have said before, the President has multiple tools in his toolbox. Let's be clear about what today's ruling was and what it wasn't. Despite the misplaced gloating from Democrats, ill-informed media outlets, and the very people who gutted our industrial base, the court did not rule against President Trump's tariffs. Six justices simply ruled that IEEPA authorities cannot be used to raise even one dollar of revenue. This administration will invoke alternative legal authorities to replace the IEEPA tariffs. We will be leveraging Section 232 and Section 301 tariff authorities that have been validated through thousands of legal challenges since the first Trump term. Treasury's estimates show that the use of Section 122 authority, combined with potentially enhanced Section 232 and Section 301 tariffs, will result in virtually unchanged tariff revenue in 2026. And with that, I look forward to chatting with Ray Washburne and taking your questions. Thank you. Please welcome Ray Washburne to the stage. Well Scott, first of all, thank you for coming. SCOTUS has &#39;taken away&#39; Trump&#39;s leverage: Bessent

Fox News(14.9M) ·19:40 ·285.8K views · 2026-02-20

Despite a Supreme Court ruling limiting the President's "instantaneous leverage" under the IEEPA, the Treasury Department plans to maintain its tariff and revenue targets using alternative trade authorities and the "Draconian" power of full embargoes.
TL;DR Despite a Supreme Court ruling limiting the President's "instantaneous leverage" under the IEEPA, the Treasury Department plans to maintain its tariff and revenue targets using alternative trade authorities and the "Draconian" power of full embargoes. Loss of IEEPA...

TL;DR

Despite a Supreme Court ruling limiting the President's "instantaneous leverage" under the IEEPA, the Treasury Department plans to maintain its tariff and revenue targets using alternative trade authorities and the "Draconian" power of full embargoes.

Trade Strategy and the Post-SCOTUS Legal Pivot

  • Loss of IEEPA Authority: Secretary Bessent characterized the Supreme Court’s ruling against the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA) as a "setback" that removes the President's ability to apply immediate, customized leverage against trading partners.
  • Alternative Mechanisms: The administration intends to use a tiered approach to maintain current tariff levels:
      • Section 122: Allows for an immediate 10% global tariff within three days to address balance-of-payment deficits.
      • Sections 232 and 301: The Commerce Department and USTR will launch investigations to extend or implement further tariffs (estimated 30–90 day timeline).
  • The "Embargo" Leverage: Bessent noted that while the Court restricted certain tariff collections, it reaffirmed the President's right to a full embargo. He suggests this "all-or-nothing" power provides a more severe, albeit less flexible, negotiating tool.
  • Revenue and Refunds:
      • Treasury expects no revenue decrease for the 2026 fiscal year.
      • Regarding the $130B–$175B in previously collected tariffs, Bessent expects years of litigation and warned that court-ordered refunds would constitute "corporate welfare" for importers and foreign suppliers.

The "3-3-3" Economic Framework

  • Core Targets: The administration is operating under a "3-3-3" policy goal: 3% GDP growth, a 3% federal deficit, and an increase of 3 million barrels of oil production per day.
  • Deficit Reduction: Bessent aims to lower the deficit from its current 5-6% range to 3% by the end of the term.
      • Strategy: "Restrain the numerator and grow the denominator" by limiting spending increases to 2-3% while achieving nominal growth of 5.7%.
  • Labor and Wages: The Secretary emphasized that real wages have outpaced inflation every month of the current term, contrasting this with the 21.5% cumulative inflation during the Biden administration.

"Trump Accounts": Universal Wealth Building

  • Policy Innovation: Bessent described "Trump Accounts" as a merger of Wall Street and Main Street, aimed at the 38% of American families with no stock market exposure.
  • Account Structure:
      • The Treasury will seed every child born in the U.S. with $1,000 in an index fund.
      • Funds are locked until age 18 and can be used for home down payments, education, or rolled into retirement accounts.
  • Philanthropic Support: Texans Susan and Michael Dell have reportedly committed $6.25 billion to support the initiative.

Personal Background and Administration Philosophy

  • George Soros Association: Bessent addressed his 15-year tenure with George Soros, clarifying that he worked strictly on the investment side rather than the political foundation. He noted he has not spoken with Soros since 2016 and benefited from market movements when Soros "lost several billion dollars" betting against Trump’s first election.
  • Merit-Based Governance: As a high-ranking gay official, Bessent emphasized that President Trump selects cabinet members based on merit rather than identity or background.
  • Gavin Newsom Conflict: Bessent dismissed recent criticisms from California Governor Gavin Newsom, describing the governor as having a "glass glass" and "slow brain" when challenged in policy debates.

Notable Quotes

"The Supreme Court has taken away the President's leverage, but in a way, they have made the leverage that he has more Draconian because they agreed he does have the right to a full embargo." — Scott Bessent
"If [fentanyl tariffs] is not an emergency authority, what was?" — Scott Bessent
"President Trump created the cabinet not because of who you go home to at night, not because of the color of your skin... he created it based on merit." — Scott Bessent
"We can grow our way out of this if we restrain the numerator and grow the denominator." — Scott Bessent

What Peter Thiel has to do with Germany’s drones | Berlin Briefing Podcast

DW News(6.2M) ·32:16 ·13.1K views · 2026-02-20
Peter Thiel (Entrepreneur and Investor)
Germany is fast-tracking a multi-billion euro combat drone deal with startups Helsing and Stark Defense to modernize the Bundeswehr, but the involvement of controversial U.S.
TL;DR Germany is fast-tracking a multi-billion euro combat drone deal with startups Helsing and Stark Defense to modernize the Bundeswehr, but the involvement of controversial U.S. investor Peter Thiel in Stark Defense has ignited a fierce political debate over national...

TL;DR

Germany is fast-tracking a multi-billion euro combat drone deal with startups Helsing and Stark Defense to modernize the Bundeswehr, but the involvement of controversial U.S. investor Peter Thiel in Stark Defense has ignited a fierce political debate over national security, data privacy, and democratic values.

The Strategic Necessity for Loitering Munitions

  • A Shift in Warfare: The Russian invasion of Ukraine has fundamentally altered military strategy, with drones—specifically loitering munitions—becoming a decisive factor.
      • Casualty Data: According to Roman Goncharenko (DW), between 60% and 80% of all casualties in the current conflict are caused by drones rather than traditional artillery.
      • Capability Gap: Germany is currently "catching a train that is already moving," having previously been reluctant to invest in armed drones due to domestic political opposition from the Social Democrats.
  • Technical Specifications: The current contracts focus on loitering munitions, which act as a hybrid between a drone and a cruise missile.
      • Range and Deployment: These systems can fly for approximately 100 kilometers and are launched vertically or via catapult to strike targets behind enemy lines.
      • Stockpile Requirements: Unlike high-cost cruise missiles (like the Taurus), which are kept in limited quantities, modern warfare requires these munitions in the thousands.

The "Zeitenwende" in Procurement Culture

  • Modernizing Defense Contracts: Christopher Schuetze (NYT) notes a significant shift in how the Bundeswehr operates, moving away from 30-year procurement cycles toward agile, framework-based contracts.
      • Initial Investment: The current deal is worth approximately €536 million initially, with potential scaling into the billions.
      • Innovation Centers: The establishment of centers like the drone hub in Erding shows a new collaboration between "young guys in white sneakers" (tech startups) and traditional army officers.
  • The "Unicorn" Potential: Startups like Helsing (founded 2021) and Stark Defense (founded 2024) are scaling rapidly. Stark is approaching "unicorn" status (a $1 billion valuation) following its most recent funding rounds.

The Peter Thiel Political Controversy

  • Political Alarm: Lawmakers, particularly from the Green Party, are concerned about Peter Thiel’s influence as a stakeholder in Stark Defense.
      • Ideological Clashes: Sara Nanni (Green Party defense expert) highlighted Thiel’s "radical views," citing his public skepticism regarding the compatibility of freedom and democracy.
      • Palantir Precedent: Thiel’s co-founding of Palantir, which already has a controversial footprint in German police technology, exacerbates fears over data privacy and civil liberties.
  • Official Response: Defense Minister Boris Pistorius initially downplayed the risk, suggesting Thiel’s stake was only between 3% and 4.5%, though he later conceded that the role of the investor must be investigated to ensure German intellectual property and decision-making remain secure.

Industry Competition and Risk Management

  • De-risking the Supply Chain: Emil Archambault (DGAP) explains that the Ministry of Defense is intentionally splitting contracts between multiple companies to avoid "putting all eggs in one basket."
      • Supplier Redundancy: By partnering with both Helsing and Stark, the Bundeswehr ensures that if one supplier faces supply chain issues or bankruptcy, the military capability remains intact.
  • Legacy Manufacturers Bypassed: Traditional German defense giants like Rheinmetall are not part of this specific drone contract, as they are still in the development phase for similar products.
  • Capital Shortages: A primary reason German startups look to controversial U.S. investors like Thiel is the historic reluctance of German banks and state funds to invest in weapons development.

Notable Quotes

"Germany is too late on this drone market... it is no longer artillery. Sixty to eighty percent of all casualties on both sides are caused by drones." — Roman Goncharenko, DW Eastern Europe Service
"Peter Thiel is a different story. He has very radical views on society... He says freedom and democracy are incompatible. And I don’t know what the role of the German defense industry in Peter Thiel’s political motives is going to be." — Sara Nanni, Green Party Defense Expert
"This is essentially a big part of the *Zeitenwende*... this realization that we can’t order things the way we used to order things, which was like, 'in thirty years we’ll get some new helicopters.'" — Christopher Schuetze, New York Times Berlin Correspondent
"It is not enough to have small stockpiles of very high-level or high-quality munitions... in Ukraine, we see you need several hundreds of those a day if not thousands." — Emil Archambault, German Council on Foreign Relations (DGAP)
Hi and welcome to Berlin Briefing. So today we’re unpacking why a German drone deal has suddenly turned into a political firestorm. The Bundeswehr, the German military, wants to buy combat drones from two startups: one is Helsing, a company based in Munich, and the other one is Stark Defense. But, and here comes the catch, one of Stark's key backers is US billionaire Peter Thiel. That alone wouldn't be explosive, except Thiel is known for right-libertarian views, his skepticism towards liberal democracy, and his closeness to Donald Trump. All of that has German lawmakers alarmed. Add to that his role as co-founder of Palantir, whose police tech footprint in Germany has already sparked heated civil liberties debates. So today, with three sharp guests, we’ll dig into the question at the heart of this: what happens when one of America's most controversial tech investors becomes a stakeholder in Germany's next generation of military hardware? Parliament is supposed to vote on those drone deals next week. Will they give their green light? I’m very excited to hear the predictions from my guests. I’ve got Christopher Schuetze here with me, Berlin correspondent for the New York Times. You cover defense and how the new geopolitical realities shape German society. We've been on the odd trip with the German defense minister together, and that is of course also something that Emil Archambault watches very, very closely. You're a drone expert essentially with the German Council on Foreign Relations. Great to have you. Thank you very much for the invitation. Yes. That's brilliant. And we’ve got somebody else who knows his drones, and we’re talking about my colleague Roman Goncharenko from DW’s Eastern Europe service. You have been speaking to a lot of experts. You and I were at the Munich Security Conference. By the way, we also did a special episode of Berlin Briefing last week, so check that out. We’ll post the link again in the comments. But yeah, Roman, great to have you on board. Maybe first question to you: why does Germany need these two deals with those companies? Well, Germany is trying to catch a train that is already moving. Germany is too late on this drone market worldwide. I mean, both the production in Germany and the weapons for the Bundeswehr, the German army. The Russian war against Ukraine has shown that drones are extremely important. They are a game changer. According to the recent information that we have, sixty to eighty percent of all casualties on both sides are caused by drones. So it is no longer artillery. I remember debates in Germany a few years ago before the Russian invasion in 2022 when Germany was very reluctant to buy drones. Especially the Social Democrats were against drones that can be used as a weapon. So the debate was: can we buy them, should we buy them? And the answer was rather no, let’s just buy reconnaissance drones. But times have changed and now Germany is catching up. It has actually no time because Germany is now beefing up or building its tank brigade in Lithuania on NATO's eastern flank, and Germany needs those drones. It's loitering munition basically—drones that can hang in the air for a long time, travel a long distance up to one hundred kilometers, and then strike the target. Germany needs drones, such loitering munition drones, to protect that brigade. Okay, that's a great summary for why these two deals are now on the table. And Emil, if I can ask you, what exactly are we talking about? Also, yes, these drones as Roman said, it is something that has been really ramping up since the beginning of the war, the 2022 war in Ukraine. It is important to note that there are several types of drones and what we're talking about here, as Roman said, is loitering munition. So it is not a drone like the ones that were debated in the 2010s that carry missiles or that carry bombs. It is not also the small handheld drones that are perhaps thirty, forty, fifty centimeters across—the sort of FPV drones, first person video drones, that are used really in close combat in Ukraine. What we're talking about here is roughly the size of very small airplanes, perhaps a few meters across, that can fly for about a hundred kilometers. They’re either launched vertically or from a catapult. They’re essentially one-way munitions. So it is something of a hybrid between a drone, a cruise missile, and perhaps a large piece of artillery. They’re meant to hit targets that are not immediately at the frontline but behind the frontlines. What that means is that you need big stocks of them. What we’ve seen, and this is something that all Western militaries have realized since the beginning of the war in Ukraine, is that it is not enough to have small stockpiles of very high level or high quality munitions. For instance, Germany has been using the cruise missile Taurus that is very expensive, very capable. It’s an excellent munition. It’s very heavy, but Germany has stocks currently of a few hundred and a few hundred more on order. That is not enough. In Ukraine we see you need several hundreds of those a day, if not thousands a day. And therefore you need very large stocks of these munitions. That is what is at issue here. The Bundeswehr needs to buy a lot of them and especially build up a production capability to build more of them to be ready if they ever need to use them. And what is special about the two contracts that are on the table, where lawmakers are expected to vote next week in Bundestag, is that the Bundeswehr would for the first time essentially sign contracts with companies and say look, we believe that you’ve got some great potential here. Because the technology is moving so fast, we just rely on you as a partner to come up with great new solutions whenever the technology evolves. Right, Chris? Just describe how much the mindset has also changed please in terms of Bundeswehr procurement. Well I think, you know, this is essentially a big part of the Zeitenwende—of this change of era. This is a response and a realization that we can’t order things the way we used to order things, which was like, you know, in thirty years we’ll get some new helicopters. This is how things were three years ago and they’re trying to change it, especially because as you say the technology is changing so quickly. If you were to order something now and you’d order it for ten years from now or five years from now, that technology would be totally outdated. Well, the Zeitenwende must be financed. All these new companies—and when we’re talking about Stark, that is a company that was founded in 2024. It’s a very young company. Helsing was founded in 2021, Quantum was founded in 2015. We're talking about very young companies that are perhaps just entering the scale-up stage. These companies have very high needs for capital to be able to develop products, test those products, and field those products. That means that they are taking a lot of risk. That is not something that German funding bodies generally have been willing to engage with. There has been a great reluctance by state investors to invest in anything that touches weapons development. There has been a great reluctance by banks or funds with strong rules on social investment guidelines to invest in anything that touches defense. That is changing now, but that was perhaps not the case when these companies were founded. Therefore, where does the money come from? It is a problem, and it may be the case that they need to find the money somewhere, and Germany was not historically a place where there was a lot of venture capital available for defense investment, particularly when it comes to weapons development. Two short remarks if I may. I’d like to play an advocate for the German Defense Ministry because I do believe that they would like to have other competitors, but they don’t have them. The central idea was to have something quick and something that is German-made. And there are only two companies that can do that. Rheinmetall is still developing. They want to split the contract and have two competitors, but I think that others will come on at a later stage and the share of Stark Defense, for example, could drop and someone else could come in. So things are developing very fast. And one more remark is the whole story of how Germany is handling this reminds me of the German car industry. For years, they were very reluctant to develop electric cars and were developing diesel engines because they were so good. The result was that the competitors from abroad were faster, like Tesla or the Chinese manufacturers. To me, it looks like a very similar situation now in drone development. Germany has to move fast; it has the money now, so we will see rapid changes in development and procurement in Germany in the coming years. Okay Emil. Yeah, I think that is entirely right that Germany needs to move fast. As Roman said, this is a clear capability gap that has been identified and therefore the need to invest in this area. I think there is also a clear desire now on the part of the government to not put all the eggs in one basket. There is a deliberate effort to de-risk procurement by looking at several products at the same time. As you mention, those are products that are still in development. They are being fielded partially in Ukraine, but these are still development products. There is a hope and there is potential that these will be exported for Stark and for Helsing. Ultimately, if Rheinmetall or other companies enter the field, these will be very good systems for the Bundeswehr. But it is still an objective to not only rely on one manufacturer. That might be how before the Zeitenwende Germany or Western militaries might have done it—identifying a competition and deciding Company A has the best product, therefore we will buy Company A. That is not how it’s working now. The objective is to have multiple ones, which means that if one of them for whatever reason stops producing or has problems with supply, quality, or goes bankrupt, it is not a critical loss for the capabilities of the armed forces. Therefore, drawing on Stark makes sense. It is a product that, so far as we can tell, the Ministry of Defense thinks has the potential to fulfill the need.
